Zimbabwe in November-December 1990, comprising Messrs/Mesdames R. Hecht
(Sr. Economist, mission leader), J. de Beye- (Economist), A. Hill (Sr.
Demographer), D. Sebina (Public Hea.th Speclalist), B. Jacobson
(consultant, Architect), and Y. Ad'A Boahene (consultant, Health Management
Specialist). Messrs. S. Sinding (Population Advisor) and A. Berg
(Nutrition Advisor) served as the lead advisors. Ms. R. Mendoza provided
secretarial support in the preparation of the report. Mr. D. de Ferranti
and Mr. S. Denning are she managing Division Chief and Department Director,

ZIMBABWE
SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
Loan and Project Summary
Borrowers          Republic of Zimbabwe
Beneficiaries:    Ministry of Health
Zimbabwe National Family Planning Council
Loan Amount:       US$ 25 million
Termss             Repayable in 20 years, including 5 years of grace, at the
standard variable rate.
Proiect Obiectives:
The project will provide the required financial and human
resources for most of the Government's five-year investment
program (1992-96) for health/population/nutrition (H/P/N),
and wfll promete policy and institutional reforms, in order
tot k..) improve maternal and child health and nutrition
status; (b) reduce the rate of population growth; and (c)
ensure that households in the 16 worst-served districts (40Z
of the population) have access to basic H/P/N services. To
achieve these objectives, the project will increase the
output ard effective utilization of trained Zimbabwean
health, famAIy planning, and nutrition workers; enhance MOH
capacity to plan and manage H/P/N activities; and increase
efficiency and mobilize additional resources.
Project Descriptions
The project will support the following six components: (a)
Family Plannings training, community outreach and static
facility services, special youth services, IEC campaigns,
evaluation and research, and better management; (b) Maternal
and Child Health: midwifery training, maternity equipment,
school health program, and operations research; (c)
Nutrition: supplementary food production, growth monitoring.
micro-nutrient supplementation, IEC, and training; (d) Rural
Health Delivery: upgrading of 16 district hospitals and
about 80 RHCs, provision of staff housing, ambulances and
supervisory transport, medical equipment, and radio
communication; (e) Health Manpower Development:
institutional strengthening, curriculum reform, systems
development and studies, and selective upgrading of training
facilities; and (f) Health Management Strengthening:
training and systems development in financial management,
materials/equipment management, district-level information
systems, and sector planning.



- iii -
Justification and Risks:
The proposed project will directly benefit low income
households in rural and urban areas, especially women and
children, by improving their health and nutrition status.
The roughl7 800,000 women expected to utilize modern family
planning methods in 1995 will obtain a wider range of
methods, better quality services, and easier access, while a
large proportion of the 250,000 women giving birth in 1995
will experience safer pregnancies and childbirth. The two
million persons living in the 16 target districts will
benefit from expanded hospital and health center-based
services. Targetted communities will learn how to monitor
the growth of all under-fives, and to act to protect those
at nutritional risk. Large numbers of Zimbabwean doctors,
nurses, and other paramedical cadres will acquire additional
skills and increased incomes as a result of project-funded
training. The technical and allocative efficiency of the
national health and family planning system will be
significantly enhanced as a result of management
improvements, and extra resources for health will be
generated through increased cost recovery and a widening of
medical insurance coverage. For Zimbabwe as a whole, better
health and nutrition and lower population growth will yield
benefits in the form of increased labor productivity,
improved educability of children, and lower outlays, oublic
and private, for social services to a smaller population.
The main risks are: (a) weaknesses in the local construction
industry could delay the hospital and RHC upgrading program;
and (b) inadequate institutional capacity in M0H and other
Government agencies could prevent timely project
implementation. The first risk will be minimized by
staggering the construction schedule and dividing the main
civil works contracts among several firms. Also, the
overall trade and investment liberalization process in
Zimbabwe should help revitalize the construction industry.
The Government will aim to mitigate the second risk by
implementing the new scheme of public service for Zimbabwean
doctors, providing additional incentives to health workers
serving in the rural areas (including project-financed staff
housing), and expanding and reorienting training programs to
emphasize community medicine and public health. The health
management, family planning, and nutrition components of the
project are all designed to develop skills and systems
required to improve the planning, execution, and evaluation
of H/P/N programs.

ZIMBABWE
SECOND FAMILY HEALT! PROJECT
STAF APPRAUISAL REPORT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      While economic performance has been uneven in the 10 years since
Zimbabwe gained its independence in 1980 (with rapia grcwth in the early
and last years of the decade, and stagnation during the middle period),
Zimbabwe has macu impressive and consistent progress in the health,
population, and nutrition sectors. As a result of concerted Government
policy decisions to (a) reorient expenditures from upper-income urban
hcuseholds to poor rural families, (b) focus health and nutrition services
upon primary and preventive care, and (c) support strongly at the highest
political levels the national goal reducing the rate of population growth
through child-spacing and fertility reduction measures, dramatic changes
have occurred during the past decade. Infant mortality has declined from
over 100 per 1000 live births to 53 in 1990 and life expectancy haa
increased from 56 to 63 years during the same period. Prevalence of severe
malnutrition in children has fallen from 022 to 12?. Use of modern methods
of contraception has risen from about 14? in 1960 to an estim&vted 362 in
1988, the highest level in Sub-Saharan Africa.
1.02      Even though this progress is important and praiseworthy, much
more needs to be done if Zimbabwe is to achieve its goal of providing
access to good-quality preventive and curative care to all of the country's
nine million inhabitants, as stated in the Government's recently updated
'Equity in Health* white paper. Significant problems remains maternal
morbidity and mortality rates are unacceptabl" high; rural dwellers in meny
districts still do not have ac_;ess to a well-staffed and equipped rural
health center (RHC) or district referral hospital; the family planning
program is heavily based on oral contraceptives, with low availability of
other methods, and current outreach and promotional efforts appear to be
inadequate for Zimbabwe to complete the transition to lower fertility over
the next 5-10 years; planning and management systems, especially those for
financial and materials/equipment management, are weak and result in
uznecessary inefficiency and wastage.
1.03      At the same time, Zimbabwe is about to enter a period of
economic adjustment which will entail certain austerity measures and cuts
in public expenditures. Under these circumstance, it is even more urgent
that the Government ensure that in the human resources sectors: (a) poor
and vulnerable households are shielded from the negative social effects of
adjustment; (b) allocative and technical efficiency is increased so that
maximum benefits are derived from tightly constrained budgets; and (c) more
financial resources are mobilized, especially by stimulating private and
N';O activities in health, population, and nutrition.
1.04      The proposed Second Family Health Project (FHP2) will make a
major contribution to the realization of Zimbabwe's sectoral goals for



healt'h, population, and nutrition over the decade of the 1990s. As the
centerpiece of the Government's five-year investment program for 1991-95,
FHP2 will assist Zimbabwe in achieving its equity, efficiency. and quality-
of-life improvement objectives, while simultaneously protecting low-income
households from the adverse social consequences of economic adjuctment. It
will do so by promoting a major further expansion of family planning and
related fo-rtility reduction; improving maternal health and child nutrition
programs; building, staffing, and equipping rural health faci'lcies in the
most deprived districts if the country; boosting the output and quality of
various cadres of basic health workers; and strengthening critical
management systems for financial resources, personnel, and physical assets.
1.05      Based upon the suk:essful experience to date with the first
Family Health Project (Ln 2)44-ZIM), the Government and World Bank agreed
in early 1989 to prepare a second project that would build upon the first
operation. The Ministry of Health (MOH) subsequently took in hand and
directed the preparation of FHP2, starting in mid-1989. The Government and
donors can be fairly optimistic that the proposed project will be
effectively implemented, because the local, broad-bRsed effort to prepare
the project ha3 already demonstrated clearly the strong degree of
Zimbabwean commitment to project objectives and activities.



II. SECTOR STATUS AND ISSUES
A. Poulatin
2.1         Population Dynamics  Zimbabwe's current population (mJd 1990)
is estimated at around 10 million, giving an overall densi&y of 25 persons
per ihm2, spread very unevenly across the country. Urbanization was 262 in
1982 but has lncreased substantially since then. Most urban dwellers liv6
in Harare, Chitungwiza or Bulawayo.  Life expectaney has .oeen over 50 for
the last 30 years and is now (1990) estimated at 64 years, among the
hlghest in Africa. Only about 82 of children die du7ing their first five
years of life.
2.2         Population growth in Zimbabwe was extremely high for decades,
fuelled by very high fertility and relatively low and falling mortality.
It is now declining as a result of recent falls in fertility. The total
fertility rate (TFR) is estimated to have fallen from around 8 before 1970
to 5.7 by 1936 and to less than 5 at present. Modern contraceptive
prevalence has risen from 52 in the late 1960s to 362 in 1988. The rate of
population growth has consequently fallen from close to 42 in the 19609 to
3.22 in the late 19808.  Future trends in population size and growth will               r
depend primarily on the speed with which fertility continues to decline.
2,3         Population Policies  The Government of Zimbabwe has long been
concerned over the harmful impact of high fertility ar.d pid population
growth on the country's socio-economic development ard on family welfare.
Particular concerns have includedt pressure on natural resources, the
problem of absorbing a rapidly-growing labor force in constrained
traditional agricultural and modern employment sectors; pressure on
government social service budgets from the youthful and rapidly-increasing
population; and the inability of women to contribute their maximum efforts
to development under the burden of excessive childbearing.
2.4         These considerations have led to a number of statements and
actions by Government aimed at reducing rapid population growth. Most
important has been strong and continued commitment to family planning in
Zimbabwe: notably the creation of the Zimbabwe National Family Planning
Council (ZNFPC) through a 1985 Act of Parliament. More recently, steps
have been taken towards the design and adoption of a national population
policy, with active participation from parliamentarianis, the main political
party (ZANU-PF), the Cabinet, and MOH/ZNFPC. Proposals have been submitted
to the President and Cabinet for the establishment of a Secretariat for
Population Policy and Development that would oversee formulation of a
policy and an implementation program, and are at present under
consideration.
Family Planning Program Services
2.5         The ZNFPC is the third incarnation of a Family Planning
Association originally established in 1957. It is a parastatal
organization under the Ministry of Health, governed by an 18-member Board



- 4 -
chaired by th,e Permanent Secretary of M0, with the Vice-Chair allocated to
the Permanent Secretary of the Ministry of Community Development. The
mandate of ZNFPC is to bring family planning (PP) services and information
to persons in need oZ them, with responsibility for all FP training and
contraceptive commodity distribution in the country (excep' for some items
sold commercially through the retail pharmacy network).
2.6         ZNFPC has its headquarters in Harare and administrative offices
in the eight provincial capitals. It is headed by the Executive Director,
assisted by two Deputy Directors and six Unit Heads. There are three
service support and management units: the Medical/Clinical Unit, the
Community-Based Distribution (CBD) Unit and the Youth Advisory Services
(YAS) Unit; and three technical support unitst Training, Information,
Education and Communication (IEC), and Evaluation and Research (ERU). A
seventh unit, Management and Administration is run by the Deputy Director
for Administration and Finance, to whom the eight Provincial Managers
Province report. ZNFPC organization is shown in Annex 2, Part 1.
2.7         Each Provincial Manager is solely responsible for all ZNFPC
program activities within that province. Every ZNFPC provincial office has
counterparts of the three service units -- Medical/Clinical, CBD and YAS --
as well as an Accounts Section; however, these only send information to the
corresponding central units and do not report to them. The three technical
support units hiave no provincial counterpart.
2.8         Total ZNFPC staff strength is somewhat over 1,000, of whom
about a tenth are in Harare (including 30 professionals). About three
quarters of ZNFPC szaff are involved in the CBD system through either
providing or supervising CBD field services.
2.9         The ZNFPC offers three major types of FP service d-rectly, and
these account for just under half of all FP users in Zimbabwe; namely
Medical/Clinical, CBD and YAS services. Medical/Clinical services are
offered through ZNFPC's network of 33 fixed-location and five mobile FP
clinics. The fixed clinics serve urban centers (there is one in every
provincial capital), but also function as back-up and referral centers for
the CBD network. They provide a wide range of FP methods, including IUDs,
injectables (on strict health-related criteria) and female barrier methods,
as well as pills and condoms. The two clinics in Harare and Bulawayo also
provide infertility treatment, cervical and breast cancer screening, and
perform sterilizations; other clinics refer clients to hospital for such
services. The mobile clinics (two in Manicaland, two in Midlands and one
in Masvingo) serve areas without access to fixed health or ZNFPC clinics,
and which hence could not otherwise be serviced by CBDs; such areas are
generally either remote, difficult of access or given over to commercial
farms. They provide clinical back-up for CBDs, a range of PP methods
including IUDs and some other services such as cervical smears.
2.10        The CBD network is the only family plannir.g outreach system in
Zimbabwe and is the backbone of the country's FP services. CBDs are rural
FP outreach workers, working in and selected by their own community. They
visit households to motivate, provide an initial supply of pill cycles and,



-5-
after an initial exam (plus subsequent regular check-ups at a ZNFPC clinic
or other health facility), resupply with pills. CBDs also dispense condoms
and refer couples to ZNFPC or health facilities for other PP methods if
desired. Numbers of CBDs (currently at 668) have remained fairly stable
for several years; the system was reported in 1990 to cover about three-
quarters of the rural population, with very high coverage of households
within CBD areas (a radius of around 20 km around the CBD's home).
2.11        Youth Advisory Services are aimed at the young and single
population and conslst largely of education and counselling services. The
core service is Family Life Education (FLE), now being provided by 23 Youth
Advisors, on invitation only, to nearly 850 schools plus some groups of
young people not in school and some parent groups. FLE is given three
times in schools, to Grade 7 pupils (final Primary year), Form 2 pupils,
and Form 4 pupils (the last Secondary year for many), as well as to older
groups in tertiary education or elsewhere: it includes, according to age of
audience, basic information on human reproduction, education on puberty and
the need to avoid pregnancy, and FP information and motivation. Other YAS
activities include a weekly radio program and a Youth Advisory Center in
Harare for confidential counselling of young people, including PP advice if
desired. YAS services are estimated to have reached up to 1.5 million
students and 200,000 adults over the past few years.
2.12        Rather more than half of all FP users in Zimbabwe do not obtain
services directly from ZNFPC. Most of these are clients of the government
(MOH, MLGRUD and City Health Department) network of health facilities,
although some use private and/or company health facilities. In all cases,
however, FP training is provided by ZNFPC, either directly, through
training of health staff at ZNFPC training centers, or indirectly, through
ZNFPC training of FP trainers. ZNFPC also receives and distributes all
contraceptive commodities entering Zimbabwe, except for a few commercially
sold items.
2.13        Since 1986, it has been MOH policy to integrate FP services
into general MCH and PHC services. About 2,500 MOH/MLGRUD/City Health
staff and 5,400 others (community leaders and traditional midwives) have
received FP training at varying levels of specialization. A wide range of
methods is available in those hospitals which have suitable and adequate
facilities and suitably-trained staff, but in most lower-level facilities
nurses are trained only to dispense pills and condoms in much the same
manner as CBDs. Some FP motivation is done by the former Village Health
Workers, now being re-trained as multi-purpose Village Community Workers.
2.14        Family Planning Training  The ZNFPC Training Unit manages all
in-country training of medical, paramedical, and lay personnel in FP. It
runs two centers in Harare and Bulawayo, and is staffed by 11 Senior
Tutors. Courses provided include a 4-week course on all aspects of FP
methods and service delivery for doctors and nurses; a 3-week practical
course on IUD insertion; a 4-week course for training of trainers; a pre-
service 6-week course for CBDs; and in-service short training or refresher
courses for all ZNFPC workers.



- 6 -
2.15        In order to cope with the training of large numbers of MO0
personnel needed to implement the 1986 decision to integrate FP into health
services, FHP1 helped establish an additional health/FP training center in
each of the eight project districts. These serve as provincial centers for
MOH/MLGRUD staff training. They are staffed by MOH trainers Y'rr are
trained and provided with technical supervision and back-up b) _aFPC.
There is also a limited amount of specialized training abroad, most notably
of doctor and nurse teams in mini-laparotomy sterilization at specialist
centers in Kenya.
2.16        Family Planning INC Program and Strategy  The ZNFPC's IEC Unit
is responsible for developing national FP IEC plans, strategies and
materials, and for handling ZNFPC's press and public relations. It is
staffed by three permanent professionals; there are no trained IEC
personnel in the ZNFPC provincial offices. An Audio-Visual Production Unit
has recently been completed under FHP1 funding in the ZNFPC grounds; ZNFPC
shares it with MOH. Production of materials in other media is contracted
uUt.
2.17        In its early years, the IEC Unit took a low-key approach and
concentrated on ZNFPC public relations and basic materials production,
focussing largely on building general awareness of family planning and on
the health benefits of child spacing. The ZNFPC's 1987-91 IEC Plan and
Strategy aimed at more assertive and broad-based IEC and more specific
messages for each target group. The plan includeds changing the use of
mass media from public relations to FP information and support; intensive
use of interpersonal communication; strengthening FP provider skills; use
of community-level workers for motivation and referral; and forming
community-level groups for PP promotion. As part of this plan, the IEC
Unit has just completed a broad-based male motivation campaign, and has
chosen IEC for service providers as its next major area of attention.
2.18        Family Planning Monitoring, Evaluation, and Research  The ERU
Unit is responsible for all FP service statistics in the country and for
research to evaluate program impact. It has six professional posts plus
(until recently) a resident expatriate adviser, and full computer
facilities for MIS management, ZNFPC personnel files and payroll. Recent
studies include surveys of CBD performance and attitudes to IUDs among
providers, clients and public; a pilot study of the expansion of the CBD
outreach network through the use of contraceptive depot holders is planned
to begin shortly. The MIS system is ir. disarray at present. An intended
streamlining of reporting procedures for clinic and health facilities is
still not operational, with several different reporting forms still in use.
MOH's system to collect, collate and convey reports from its facilities to
the ERU is not working either, with no more than a quarter of them
reporting regularly. Only the separate CBD MIS system is working
efficiently. As a result, routine VP statistics have become completely
unreliable for tracking progress, program impact, trends or problems,
forcing an almost total dependence on periodic sample surveys of which the
latest was the 1988 Zimbabwe Demographic and Health Survey (ZDHS) and its
1989/90 follow-up Zimbabwe Service Availability Survey.



-7-
Population/Family Planning Financimn and External Support
2.19        No data are available on total expenditure and costs for PP
delivery through non-ZNFPC channels, due primarily to the limitations of
MOH's accounting system. ZNPPC's annual expenditure is substantial,
amounting to Z$12 million in 1988. Government has maintained and protected
strong financial support to ZNFPC through the 1980s; in all years the
largest single component of ZNFPC's income has been a MOH grant, which at
present amounts to nearly two-thirds of the total. The remainder has come
almost entirely from donors, of whom USAID has been by far the largest.
Other important donors to ZNFPC include UNFPA, IPPF (technical assistance
and commodities), Netherlands, NORAD, and GTZ (through FHPI), and UNDP.
Donor support to PP outside ZNFPC has also been substantial, the most
important sources being FHP1 (for MOH) and USAID (for the private sector).
Other aspects of population activities (such as population data collection
and development of demographic training) have also received substantial
donor support; e.g. Sweden supported the 1982 and 1992 censuses, and
various NGOs provide FP services through health facilities.
2.20        USAID Activities and Programs  USAID assisted PP development
through the bilateral US$6.5 million Zimbabwe Child Spacing and Fertility
Project between 1982 and 1985. Subsequently an even greater volume of
activities continued through a variety of centrally or regionally funded
contracts with cooperating agencies. USAID's program focus has been on
supply of contraceptive commodities and technical assistance and equipment
for specialized support activities such as IEC and research and evaluation
(the latter including the 1988 ZDHS). Most funding has gone to ZNFPC
although there have also been important programs of private-sector support
through social marketing and industrial/insurance-funded health care.
These broad directions and areas of interest will continue under the new
bilateral project, due to start late in 1990, which has been developed in
close collaboration with the Bank in order to ensure harmony with FHP2 .
2.21        USAID has prepared a new US$14 million bilateral project to run
from 1990-1995. This project, whose broad purposes are diversification of
the FP method mix and improvement of national self-reliance in the FP
field, has three components, namely Diversification of Methods, Expansion
of Private Sector Involvement, and Coordination and Sustairability
Enhancement. Planned activities include training, IEC, service delivery,
research and evaluation, private sector development and contraceptive
commodities.
2.22        UNFPA Activities and Programs  UNFPA supported a variety of
population activities through its 1983-86 Country Program whose total
budget was US$3.5 million. This program focused primarily on development
of population policy and awareness of population issues, including support
to the 1982 population census, the creation of a Population Planning Unit
in CSO, and the establishment of an undergraduate demography program at the
University. It also provided some support to MOH's MCH department, the
establishment of PP services in Harare Central Hospital and supplementary
supplies of contraceptives commodities.



2.23        An indicative second Country Program was developed in early
1989, to run from 1989 to 1993, with a total UNFPA budget of US$10 million.
The broad objectives are development of a population policy and program,
increased awareness of the impact of FP on family health and population
issues, and increased contraceptive prevalence. Within this program, sub-
projects at various states of development includes strengthening of MCH/FP
services; population, IEC and family life education for the non-formal
sector; population education in the formal school system; assistance to the
1992 population and housing census; social science research for development
planning; population policy formulation; research into law and population
issues; demographic training programs at the university; and women in
development.
2.24        World Bank Programs  The Bank, with its co-financing partners,
has provided support to FP services in the context of the government health
care network, as part of FHP2. Assistance has focussed on training
MOH/MLGRUD staff in FP, expanding urban FP services, (with an emphasis on
training and facilities for more specialized methods such as IUDs and
sterilization), and on developing and producing IEC materials, notably
through the construction (under GTZ funding) of the new Audio-Visual Unit.
The project also includes a component (funded by the Netherlands) for
strengthening FP training for community-based workers, chiefly village
health workers; however, the redeployment and lengthy re-training of this
cadre as multi-sectoral community development workers has delayed
implementation of this component.
Family Planning Program Issues
2.25        Achievements:  The achievements of Zimbabwe's family planning
program to date are impressive. Family planning has become an accepted
part of life throughout Zimbabwean society and is making a significant
impact on population dynamics. Modern contraceptive prevalence is the
highest in Sub-Saharan Africa, at 36Z of currently married women of
reproductive age (ZDHS 1988). Equally impressive is the diffusion of
contraceptive use throughout all population groups; though use is greatest
(as usual) among urban women and women with secondary education (49 and 52?
respectively), it is still relatively high among rural women (31Z) and
women with no or or / primary education (25 and 34? respectively).
Knowledge of mode- ,mily planning is practically universal among both men
and women, and approval is also very high (almost universal among women,
74Z among husbands). Motivation for FP use is increasingly shifting to a
desire to reduce family size and stop childbearing permanently rather than
simply to space births adequately for health reasons. Fertility has
already fallen by over one-third from peak levels in the late 1960s and
population growth is also now declining.
2.26        On the technical side, the quality of Zimbabwe's program is
good. The combination of ZNFPC's very ef2.ctive CBD rural outreach
network, the dense, good quality governmenL health facility network, and
ZNFPC clinics and MOH hospitals in urban areas, have provided access to
family planning services for most families in Zimbabwe. Coordination and



_ 9 "
collaboration between MOH and ZNFPC is good at central level and excellent
at field staff level. Training, supervision and contraceptive logistics
all work well. Funding for ZNFPC and MOH PP activities has never been a
problem, thanks to continued firm commitment to the program from government
and donors. User knowledge about the pill (the major method) is good and
pill discontinuation rates are low; general reported levels of client
satisfaction are high.  In many respects, therefore, Zimbabwe's program is
a model of how programs in Africa could and should work.
2.27        outstanding Needs   There remain several issues and needs to be
addressed if Zimbabwe's progress in family planning is to continue and
accelerate.  At present the program has reached a natural plateau after a
successful first stage built on a low-key approach to establishment of
family planning as a normal part of life for Zimbabwean families. This
approach was based on child spacing for maternal and child health (which
was already culturally valued) rather than on family limitation'(which was
not); it relied predominantly on the pill, a short term, easily-reversible
method suitable for short-term spacing and free from the political problems
associated with some longer term or permanent methods (injectables and
female sterilization) before Independence; and its IEC activities were
primarily channelled through interpersonal informal contacts of providers
with clients, families and communities, together with public relations for
the new ZNFPC. The CBD system, appropriately backed-up by government or
ZNFPC clinics for monitoring and referral, was an ideal vehicle for such an
approach, and has been the major reason for relatively high levels of FP
use among rural and less-educated women.
2.28        A wider and more dynamic approach is now needed to maintain and
accelerate program momentum. Key and interrelated elements would be: a
wider rationale for FP use, including child spacing and smaller family
size; a broader range of methods, including the latest versions of longer-
term or permanent methods (such as IUDs, implants and mini-lap female
sterilizations) that are suitable for long-term/indefinite spacing or
cessation of childbearing, and strengthening and broadening IEC activities
through a combination of effective client/provider personal contacts,
promotion by a variety of community-based development workers, and vigorous
mass media campaigns. For this approach to succeed, a wider range of
health personnel at all levels must be actively involved in FP service and
IEC delivery, as well as outreach workers and specialized agencies in
sectors other than health. Several moves towards such an approach have
begun - some under FHPI - but require fuller development.
2.29        Besides these shifts in tone and focus of approach, the
Zimbabwe program has now reached the stage where it can and should plan for
a substantial expansion of FP service delivery through both fixed
facilities and outreach networks. With regard to outreach, the CBD network
has never been sufficient to service the entire rural population, covering
now only around three-quarters; many of the CBDs themselves, a very stable
and low-turnover workforce, are also aging to a point where they are
becoming increasingly less mobile and productive.  While the expansion of
FP service delivery through government health facilities has partially
filled the resulting gap in coverage, there is still a need for a dedicated



- 10 -
FP outreach network for community motivation and support capable of
servicing the entire rural population. Operational research on comnunity-
based contraceptive depot-holders under CBD supervision is due soon to go
into the field, but clearly results must still be uncertain. With regard
to fixed facilities, the current expansion of FP service delivery through
government health care channels needs to continue to the point where all
health staff in both government and non-government facilities are trained,
equipped, and motivated to provide a full range of services appropriate to
their level and degree of specialization. Moreover, the linkages and
coordination between government, ZNFPC and other non-government health
facilities need to be reviewed and firmly piaced within e. comprehensive
national family planning framework, in order to avoid either duplication or
gaps in FP services.
2.30        Under such an expansion and strengthening of FP service
delivery, both funding and management of the Zimbabwe program will of
course require careful attention in order to ensure that the expanded and
strengthened program will be assured of adequate and sustainable funding
and sufficient managerial capacity. Current funding levels and patterns of
sources will need to be reviewed and augmented, and the role, structure and
functions of ZNFPC - originally set up to deal only with a relatively
small, simple and highly centralized program of its own plus back-up to a
limited number of non-ZNFPC activities - will have to be redesigned and
strengthened to service a far larger and more complex national FP program.
B. Health
2.31        Introduction to the Health Sector  Zimbabwe has made impressive
gains in providing health care to its ten million people during the decade
since independence in 1980. Progress has been especially great in
redressing the marked inequities in health care that existed prior to
independence. The Government has extended basic health care to hitherto
underserved rural areas, by constructing and staffing about 290 clinics,
reconstructing about 160 war-damaged clinics, and upgrading an additional
160 facilities. To provide preventive and promotive - and some simple
curative - services at the grass-roots level, more than 4,400 Village
Health Workers (now Community Development Workers) were trained between
1980 and 1985. To staff the clinics, large numbers of unqualified
personnel were retrained as State-Certified Nurses.
2.32        The Government's emphasis on primary health care (PHC),
combined with other measures to improve the quality of life in the rural
areas, has already started to bear fruit. The percentage of fully
immunized children increased from 25Z in 1980 to 42Z in 1984 and 67S in
1987, and could well approach the target level of 801 in 1990. Infant
malnutrition has declined from 292 in 1980 to 16? in 1984, and there is
evidence that it has continued to drop to 10-12Z in selected provinces at
present.



- 11 -
Background ou Zimbabwe's Health Sector
2.33        Health Status  The health status of Zimbabwe's 10 million
people is fairly typical for countries with a similar per capita income
(per capita GDP of US$650 in 1988) and at a similar stage of development.
Life expectancy is 64 years, infant mortality is 53 per 1000 live births,
and maternal mortality is about 90 per 100,000 deliveries in health
facilities. (There is no reliable figure for the entire population.
Although maternal mortality is low compared to other countries in the
region, there is concern that it has hardly changed since 1980.) The major
causes of mortality and morbidity among children are respiratory
infections, malrutrition, diarrhea, meningitis, measles, and malaria. The
leading causes of death and illness among adults include respiratory
diseases, tuberculosis, sexually-transmitted diseases, malaria, pregnancy-
related complications, accidents, and cardio-vascular diseases.
2.34        Sector Organization and Institutions  The institutional make-up
of Zimbabwe's health sector is highly heterogeneous, with the central
goverranent, municipalities and local authorities, church missions and other
NGos, industries and mines, private practitioners, and traditional healers
all playing significant roles. Since independence, the Government has
progressively organized the public and NGO institutions plus some private
facilities into a four-tiered system of national health service delivery,
to ensure adequate access, a balance of preventive and curative care, and
an efficient chain of patient referral:
(a)   At the first level, where the focus is on primary health care,
community involvement, and preventive health practices, there are 56
rural hospitals and 927 health centers (about 132 MOH, 510 local
government, 70 mission, 55 urban, and 160 industry-owned facilities).
(b)   The second level has 55 district hospitals (MOH and church mission-
operated), of which just 26 currently meet the standard requ.rements
for a first line of referral. Thus more than half of the district
hospitals are inadequate in terms of bed capacity, equipment,
staffing, etc., and require upgrading over the coming years. A
District Health Executive headed by the District Medical Officer and
District Nursing Officer work to integrate preventive and curative
services and to promote intersectoral collaboration.
(c)   At the third level there are eight provincial and four general
hospitals which aim to provide specialist services to deal with most
medical/surgical problems, as well as obstetrics, gynecology, and
pediatrics. A Provincial Health Executive led by the Provincial
Medical Officer is responsible for the planning and supervision of
health services throughout the province.
(d)   At the fourth level, five central hospitals (including the specialist
psychiatric hospital) located in Harare and Bulawayo, are intended to
serve as national i,ferral facilities. However, the limited
availability of secondary and tertiary care in the main cities leads
to increased workload for the central hospitals.



- 12 -
2.35        The MOH headquarters, located in Harare, provides overall
leadership, planning, policy guidance, and various kinds of support
activities (related to the management of the Ministry budget, transport,
drugs, health statistics, etc.) to the entire four-tiered health system
described above. The current organizational structure of the MOH
headquarters uas put in place in 1986-87, with substantial backing from
FH?1. It features three main wings, Health Care Services, Support Services
(each under a Principal Medical Director) and Finance and Administration
(under a Deputy Permanent Secretary); all three of these persons are
responsible to the Permanent Secre'tyy. This structure has remained in
place since the 1986-87 reorganization, except for a minor reshaping of the
Department of Htclth Service Management into a Department for Health
Planning and Management in mid-1990.
2.36        Important progress in organizational and management
strengthening has taken place over the past four years, with assistance
from FHP1 and from other donors including SIDA and WHO:
-     a national health planning system has been developed and utilized in
the preparation of the five-year plats (1991-95);
-     health services administrators have been trained in a new diploma
course and deployed to many of the districts;
-     substantial management training for middle-level managers has taken
place; and
-    management systems and procedures have been implemented for health
personnel and for related personnel information.
2.37        Despite these achievements, current health management practic-:s
continue to exhibit a number of serious weaknesses that the Government
intends to address through upcoming projects including FHP2. In
particular, skills and systems for the management of medical equipment
remain inadequate. There are major inefficiencies in the entire equipment
chain, from the identification of equipment needs and procurement of items,
to their installation and maintenance in health facilities. Equipment
maintenance personnel are in desperately short supply: of all categories of
M0H workers, the highest vacancy rate is for medical equipment technicians.
The provincial medical equipment maintenance teams are essentially non-
functional at present.
2.38        The Government now recognizes that further efforts to expand
and improve the quality of health services in Zimbabwe will require a well-
articulated health financing strategy and the financial management skills
to implement it. MOH has committed itself to four main objectives in this
area, as highlighted in the 1990 report on "Financing of Health Services in
Zimbabwe": (a) improving allocative and technical efficiency; (b)
mobilizing additional resources for health through a variety of mechanisms;
(c) continuing to enhance equity in access to health services; and (d)
strengthening financial management systems to support these goals. To



- 13 -
achieve these objectives, MOH intends to carry out a series of reforms in
health financing. In the early years, these includes
-     overhauling the MOH budget system, in part to decentralize
responsibility for budget formulation and expenditure, in part to
make the budget a more useful tool in pursuing allocative targets;
-     increasing cost recovery through user fees, by improving billing and
collection practices and raising fees while maintaining exemptions
and subsidies for low-income households; and
-     improving cost-consciousness and technical efficiency in major
hospitals, starting with the sour central hospitals.
2.39        While recent efforts to strengthen health management at
district level (e.g. through deployment of health service administrators,
enhancement of planning skills, development of in-service health worker
training plans, etc.) have been positive, much still needs to be done to
implement an integrated district health management information system
(MIS). At present, morbidity/mortality data, routine service statistics,
budget and personnel information, and reporting on inputs such as drugs,
medical supplies, and transport are kept in a number of separate and ad hoc
systems, with much of the data manually tabulated and of uneven quality. A
district-level health MIS would integrate this data in ways that would
improve its collection and analysis and use in program management. This
will become increasingly Imperative as MOH moves over the next few years to
decentralize to the district many key functions including major
responsibilities for planning and budgeting, personnel management, and the
organization of in-service training.
2.40        Health Manpower  Including personnel in both public and private
institutions, Zimbabwe has a total of about 1,250 doctors and 10,000 state-
registered nurses (SRN) and state-certified nurses and nurse-midwives
(SCN/SCMN). There are also about 700 environmental health officers and
technicians, plus about 6,000 village community workers (VCW) who were
trained as village health workers before being transferred to the Ministry
of Community and Cooperative Development as multi-purpose community
development workers. About 12,000 traditional healers are registered in a
Government-sponsored association, and their total number is believed to be
about 20,000.
2.41        Manpower is perhaps the most important resource in the
de'velopment of health services; it is also one of the most complex areas to
plau and manage. A manpower plan has recently been prepared, an important
step towards more optimal use of health staff and greater returns on the
large investments in training and staff development. In all, MOH has a
total staff establishment of 23,380, with nearly 96Z of all posts currently
filled (Annex 5, Part 1). Analysis of these data reveals thats
(a)   Nearly a third of the MOH posts are allocated to the four big central
hospitals. Parirenyatwa Hospital alone employs more staff than
Matabeleland North, Matabeleland South, and Mashonaland East



- 14 -
Provinces combined. PMD offices and rural health centers account for
12? of the total posts, and MOH Headquarters for 2.4?;
(bl   The level of overall vacancies is low, standing at 854 (42) across
all categories of staff. The restricted growth in the number of
additional posts over the last two years has resulted in a sharp drop
in the number of vacancies. There are some important exceptions,
however, including hospital equipment technicians (672 of posts
vacant), physiotherapists (42X), dieticians (36Z), dental therapists
(34Z), nutritionists (32Z), pharmacists (23?), and X-ray operators
(23Z).
(c)   Distribution of posts across the Provinces varies from 3?
(Matabeleland North) to 9.1? (Midlands), reflecting both the relative
size of the provinces and the spatial distribution of health
facilities.
2.42        A wide range of basic health training programs are provided by
the University, the Polytechnic and the Mission Hospitals as well as the
Ministry of Health (Annex 5, Part 2). For those programs run by the
University and the Polytechnic, the total number of graduates who join the
MOH (excluding those attending under cadetship) depends both on the
availability of vacant posts and upon graduate career choice. It is
therefore difficult for the MOH to control the volume of graduate
recruitment. There are no trainee posts for some categories of health
manpower trained by the MOH. In such cases (e.g., dental surgery
assistant, scientific laboratory technician), the MOH can only recruit a
new staff member to a vacant post and then train them on the job and/or
through day release courses. For a few cadres, notably those of
environmental health technicians and pharmacy technicians, training outputs
are already in excess of available posts, and the MON is wover-
establishment". In several more categories, such as radiographers and
rehabilitation assistants, training outputs will soon be beyond the MOH's
absorptive capacity.
2.43        Ideally, loss rates should be tracked by the MOH year by year
to identify trends and to be able to take action where problems seem to be
most acute. Unfortunately, historic data are not available. Even those
data that are available for 1989 are conflicting. However, an analysis of
1989 data (Annex 5, Part 3) suggests that while the turnover for a few
cadres is alarming (e.g., general doctors, pharmacists, and hospital
equipment technicians), for others the loss rate is well below the rate at
which new personnel are being trained and employed (e.g., SCNs). For those
cadres facing a net loss of personnel, MOH needs to look carefully at pay
and employment conditions, as well as other factors leading to undesirably
high rates of attrition, in order to develop effective staff retention
policies. In the other categories where training outputs exceed losses, it
will still be useful for MOH to consider the cost of improved retention
schemes, since the numbers of staff projected to leave the MOH over the
coming years represent a tremendous training investment from which MOH does
not benefit fully. For example, the approximately 1500 registered nurses
projected to leave the MON between 1991 and 1995 at the current turnover



- 15 v
rate of 7.12 represents approximately Z$11 million of training lost to the
public service.
2.44        SDecial Programs  To accelerate the development of PHC, the MOH
has launched a number of special programs, typically utilizing a core team
of full-time staff in MOH headquarters and general-purpose health workers
(nurses, VCW, etc.) in the field. These special programs includes
(a)   the Zimbabwe Expanded Program of Immunization (ZEPI), aimed at
vaccinating against the major childhood diseases. ZEPI is funded by
UNICEF, SIDA, other donors, and the Government;
(b)   the Zimbabwe Essential Drugs Action Program (ZEDAP), supported by
WHOIDANIDA. ZEDAP has published an essential drugs list for the
country, improved drug testing and safety and developed training
materials for doctors and nurses and drug prescribing practices;
(c)   rural water supply and sanitation projects have been undertaken
throughout the country, with MOH and MLGRUD taking the lead for
different parts of the overall program. Donor support for
construction of boreholes, wells, and latri.es has been forthcoming
from UNDP, DANIDA, NORAD, GTZ, the Netherlands, and the EEC;
(d)   the Zimbabwe AIDS Control Program was started in 1988 with backing
from WHO and a range of donors associated under the umbrella of the
Medium-Term Plan (MTP) for AIDS Control. Program activities to date
have covered protecting the nation's blood supply, IEC campaigns to
promote safe sex, and counselling of infected persons and their
families.
2.45        In addition, MOH runs a highly successful, well-managed
schisto-somiasis control program. Antl-malaria campaigns, featuring
chloroquine prophylaxis, are routinely mounted during the wet season in the
worst-affected areas.
Government Health Policies and SLrateties
2.46        The Government's main policies and strategies for the health
sector are spelled out in its 1984 white paper entitled 'Planning for
Equity in Health", the 1987 "Health for All Action Plan", and the 1990
updated "Equity in Health" document. The key sectoral objectives are to
improve access and equity in the provision of basic health ssrvices and to
ensure the financial sustainability of the health system. To achieve these
objectives, the Government's main programmatic strategies includes
(a)   Promoting PHC, especially in the rural areas.  This means building
and upgrading additional health centers and district hospitals And
funding priority activities including immunization, ante-natal car3,
control of diarrheal diseases, growth monitoring and therapeutic
feeding for malnourished children, health education, control of
communicable diseases, and supply of safe drinking water and
sanitation;



- 16 -
(b)   Expanding family planning, especially by integrating FP services into
the PHC activities of all health institutions, public and privatel
(c)   Improving the supply and minimizing the cost of essential drugs;
(d)   Widening access to basic health services by building or upgrading
district-level hospitals and clinics;
(e)   Improving the patient referral system through a combination of
expanded lower-level facilities, improved transport and communication
links between facilities, education, and incentives, including the
structure of fees; and
(f)   Encouraging increased efficiency and financial viability of the
health system through self-help, local financing mechanisms,
appropriate user charges, and health insurance.
ExRenditures for Health
2.47        Total expenditures on health and health-related activities
covering capital, recurrent, and technical assistance costs amounted to
ZS636.2 million in 1987. This translates to per capita expenditures of
about Z$70 or US$45 at the 1987 exchange rate. Compared to many other
developing countries, Zimbabwe's per capita expenditures for health are
relatively high. According to a 1987 survey of 29 developing countries,
Zimbabwe's per capita expenditure level of about US$30 in 1980 was one of
the highest in Sub-Saharan Africa, exceeded only by Botswana, Swaziland,
and Zambia, and was higher than a number of developing countries from other
regions including China, Indonesia, Morocco, and Thailand. About 902 cf
Zimbabwe's total health expenditures in 1987 were for operating and
maintenance purposes, and 102 for capital items. Using 1981 prices, 1987
expenditures amounted to Z$291.4 million, nearly twice the Z$155.8 million
spent on health care in 1981. This implies a real increase of almost lZ a
year over the 1981-87 period.
2.48        Providers of Health Care  Government provideR nearly two-thirds
of health care in Zimbabwe (in financial terms), but private institutions
and individuals also play a major role in the health system. In 1987, a
little more than 632 of expenditures were incurred by public providers of
health care, with the private sector accounting for the remaining 37Z.
Among the public providers, the MOH alone was responsible for two-fifths of
all health expenditures in the country (Z$255.5 million), and other
ministries involved mainly in water and sanitation improvements spent
another 132 of the total (Z$84.4 million). Municipal health departments
(dominated by Harare and Bulawayo) were also major care providers (62);
rural local government, while important in terms of coverage (about 500
clinics serving two-thirds of the population), accounted for only 2Z of
nationwide expenditures for health. The bulk of health expenditures by
private providers were incurred by private doctors (Z$156.8 million or
nearly a quarter of the total) and private industries, mines, and
commercial farms (Z$50.3 million or 8Z). The roughly 70 church mission
hospitals which account for more than a third of the 18,000 hospital beds



- 17 -
in Zimbabwe reported Z$18.6 million in expenditures or 32 of the national
total. Voluntary organizations, including the Red Cross and a number of
local NGOs, spent an additional Z$7.6 million or just over 12 of the total.
2.49        Sources of Funds  Government funds provided about 642 of the
total outlay on health (including foreign (mainly public) assistance).
Central government revenues for health amounted to Z$310.2 million or
nearly 492 of the total. These revenues financed about 90Z of the MOH
budget and, through direct subventlons, 802 of the budgets of the local
councils and church mission health 'providers. Central government funds
also made an important contribution to health-related expenditures by other
ministries (432) and municipal health departments (272).
2.50        Among the other important sources of financing for health care,
private industry, mines, end commercial farms spent over Z$50 million at
their own facilities. Voreign assistance (mainly official multi-lateral
and bilateral loans and grants) accounted for nearly 122 of expenditures,
allocated to a wide range of Government, parastatal (ZNFPC), mission, and
NGO providers. The largest share of foreign aid tZ$46 million) went to
ministries other than MOH, and was spent mainly for rural water and
sanitation. Health insurance was also a significant source of financing
for the health sector, with the 27 medical aid societies in the country
paying out Z$102 millior or nearly one-sixth of all expenditures, for
health care provided to 42 of Zimbabwe's population. Roughly 102 of
outlays for health came in the form of out-of-pocket expenditures by
private individuals, and moit of these went to private doctors and
pharmacies; direct payments by individuals covered just over lt of MOH
expenditures (2.32 when medical aid payments are included), and about a
tenth of municipalities' expenditures for health. This indicates the
relatively low level of cost recovery in the public sector.
Recent Trends in NOR Expenditures
2.51        Throughout the period 1981-88, the MOB budget has averaged
about 52 of total Government outlays, although this declined to 4.52 in the
drought year 1983 and recovered gradually to 5.42 in 1988. For middle-
income developing countries with a per capita GDP level similar to
Zimbabwe, the share of health expenditures in total government spending
varies widely, from 2-32 (Egypt, Indonesia, Morocco) to 9-112 (Dominican
Republic, Paraguay). In this regard, Zimbabwe occupies the middle ground,
with a budget share allocated to health similer to Botswana (5.62) and
slightly lower than a number of other African countries (Lesotho, Liberia,
Zambia) which spend 7-82 of the budget for health.
2.52        Between 1979-80 and 1987-88, MOH expenditures grew more than
fivefold in nominal terms (from Z$54.2 million to Z$277.3 million), by
about 942 in real terms (from Z$56.8 million to Z$110.4 million), and by
482 in real per capita terms (from Z$8.0 to Z$11.8). These significant
increasee in public spending for health reflect the post-independence
government's serious comuitment to improving access to health care for a
large segment of the population that was underserved before 1980.



- 18 -
2.53        The big surge in real HOH expenditures occurred in the first
two years following independence, when the budget increased by 37.9? and
12.52, respectively. Since then, given the overall fiscal constraints
facing the Government, the rate of real growth of the KOH budget has been
much lower, averaging 3.9Z a year during the six year period 1982-88. This
sluggish growth in public funding of health care has forco. the Government
to examine carefully ways in which available resources for health can be
more efficiently utilized, and to search for additional sources of revenues
for health services provided by HOH. The constrained state of public
finances has also prompted Government to begin considering an expanded role
for local government and the private sector (including NGOs) in providing
health care in the future.
2.54        Allocations bv Level and Tme of Service  Further analysis of
the blue book suggests that about 71? of the 1987 MOH budget (Z$171.3
million) was allocated to facility-based health services, with the balance
of funds for community-based programs (water and sanitation, disease
control campaigns, etc. - 112), training (10?), central administration
(5?), grants to ZNFPC (2Z), and research (1X).
2.55        If tne expenditures incurred by municipal and local government
health authorities using their own resources are added to the MOH budget,
then total public outlays for facility-based services come to Z$188.2
million; this is the so-called Upatient bu,'get" for Government health care.
Of this amount, nearly SO (Z$93.5 million) was spent at tertiary level,
including 36? (ZS67.4 million) at the four central hospitals and 122 (Z$21
million) at provincial hospitals.  About SOX of the patient budget (Z$56.4
million) was allocated to secondary-care ."ctlfties, including the 55
district and mission hospitals, with the rLctvaining 202 (Z$38.3 million)
spread across the roughly 1000 urban and :ctril clinics at the primary-care
level.
2.56        The distr'hution of Government ;\,-alth facilities by provin..e,
as measured by hospital beds ter 1,000 population and clinics per 10,000
population, is fairly equal.  However, the pelw capita distribution of
public financial resources for health is 2-5 ;mes higher in the two
provinces where the four central hospitals a'-i located (Hashonaland East -
Z$48, and Matabeleland North - Z$33) than in the other six provinces (Z$10-
15 per capita). Even when these figures are adjusted to take into account
patients referred from the other provinces to the central hospitals in
Harare and Bulawayo, per capita expenditures in the two "urban' provinces
are still 1.4-2.7 times hlgher than in the otheri.
2.57        This top-heavy allocation of financial resources indicates that
although much has been done since independence to correct the urban, high-
technology bias of Zimbabwe's health system (common in developing
countries), that bias still persists to some degree. Data on the average
cost per patient-day at various levels of health facilities confirm this
bias. The Government has already taken a number of positive steps to
Improve the situations (a) new construction at the central hospitals has
generally been halted since the early 19809; (b) district hospitals and
clinics are being built or upgraded to relieve pressur4 on the tertiary-



- 19 -
level facilities; and (c) MOH is implementing regulations requiring that
young doctors serve in the rural areas, while providing incentives (e.g.,
good quality housing) to health person .el to work outside of the major
towns.
Health Sector Challenges and Issues
2.58        Despite these major achievements, Government officials
recognize that many problems remain in Zimbabwe's health sector. Some
remote rural populations are still underserved; more than 100 additional
clinics are required to reach them. PHC programs to provide clean drinking
water and adequate sanitation still have a long way to go, but there is
considerable donor support for Government programs (para 2.44). Maternal
mortality rates remain unacceptably high for rural women. Preventable
communicable diseases including malaria, schistosomiasis, typhoid,
respiratory infections, tuberculosis, and sexually-transmitted diseases
(STD) continue to take a high toll in morbidity and mortality. AIDS is
also beginning to impose a significant social and economic cost.
2.59        As Zimbabwe's health care delivery system expands, the country
is also experiencing serious shortages of staff, especially in the public
sector. These include not only medical officers and nurses (especially
maternity-trained nurses), but also non-medical personnel such as
nutritionists, equipment technicians, health education officers, and health
planners and accountants. Some of these shortages are due to inadequate
training facilities; in addition, significant differentials in salary and
conditions of service between the public and private sectors are causing
high losses from the Government health service.
2.60        The Government has indicated that it will aim to maintain
spending for the health and education sectors during the current period of
economic austerity and impending structural adjustment. Nevertheless, the
severe fiscal constraints facing Zimbabwe suggest that it will be difficult
for the Government to meet its existing commitments for recurrent financing
of the health system, and also to make the additional investments in health
that are urgently needed. The country's primary health care infrastructure
and services and a range of preventive activities in particular need to be
further expanded over the coming years; additional manpower must be trained
and optimally deployed; and management systems need to be strengthened.
2.61        To address these imminent challenges, Zimbabwean policy-makers
have elected to pursue three basic health finance strategies:
(a)   Mobilize more resources for the sector, through a combination of
increases in user charges, establishment of insurance programs or
expansion of existing insurance schemes, development of earmarked
taxation, etc. The Government will also seek to induce non-
governmental institutions to assume greater responsibility for
providing and paying for health services, by encouraging church
missions, other non-profit NGOs, private practitioners, commercial
enterprises, health insurance companies, traditional healers, and



- 20 -
local communities themselves, to Increase their level. of
participation in the nation's health system;
(b)   Improve the efficiency of existing expenditures, i.e., obtain more
"value for money" in health. This means improving both allocative
and technical efficiency, especially in the public sector but also
among private health providers; and
(c)   Strengthen the management of health finances, so that public and
private health practitioners and administrators are able to design
and implement measures for enhanced resource mobilization and
efficiency. This requires the development of individual skills and
broader systems of management in financial planning, project
appraisal, expenditure control, procurement, etc.
C. Nutrition
introduction
2.62        Nutrition has been an issue of concern to the Ministry of
Health in Zimbabwe since independence. During the early years, children
under the age of five were suffering from high levels of undernutrition,
due in part to disruptions caused by the war of independence and a series
of drought years in 1982-86. Large-scale child food supplementation
programs were run during the first half of the 1980s, combined with
nutrition education. In the early 19809, the MOH Nutrition Unit also began
establishing community-based agricultural production units for feeding of
preschool children. This national program, now called Community Food and
Nutrition Program (CFNP), was run by intersectoral committees operating at
every level from national to ward. Both the 1984 and 1987 PHC evaluations
noted that nutrition was the component of PHC which had best succeeded in
achieving the Alma Ata goals of mobilizing community participation and
working intersectorally.
Nutritional Status
2.63        A review of the nutrition surveys which have been conducted
since independence and the available clinic-based nutrition surveillance
data suggests that while overall deficits in weight for age (malnutrition)
among children - the most vulnerable group - have declined somewhat during
the past decade, they are still at unacceptably high levels. Furthermore,
the gains in the overall nutrition situation have resulted mainly from
declines in acute malnutrition (wasting), with chronic malnutrition
(stunting) remaining at about the same level throughout the 10-year period.
It is estimated that malnutrition continues to account for about one-
quarter of all deaths of children aged 1-4.
2.64        Nutrition surveys conducted at the beginning and the end of the
1980s, supported by studies during the intermediate years, reflect these
trends. According to a 1982 report, about 22Z of under-fives had second or



- 21 -
third-degree malnutrition (less than 75 of median weight for age), 282
were stunted (less than 90? of median height for age), and 9? were wasted
(less than 80? of median weight for height). By 1988, the ZDHS found that
12? of the under-fives were malnourished, with 29t stunted and just 1.32
wasted. It is likely that the drop in wasting is associated with the end
of transient acute malnutrition due to the war and droughts and episodes of
acute illness among children. At the same time, the maintenance of
substantial levels of stunting is probably the result of gradual
nutritional insult to children over long periods, related to infant feeding
practices (see para. 2.72).
2.65        Low birth weight (less than 2500g), an indicator of maternal
undernutrition, was seldom measured until recently. Health facility data
show fairly low levels of 5-62 for recorded hospital-based births from 1987
to 1989. However, one urban survey found a rate of over 10?, and data are
probably biased by the fact that about a third of all deliveries do not
take place in health facilities.
2.66        A national goiter survey conducted in 1988-89 found that iodine
deficiency disorders (IDD) are a public health problem in every province of
Zimbabwe. Overall, the total goiter rate (TGR) was 4'44 and the visible
goiter rate (VGR) was 4?; TGR varied by province from 52Z in Mashonaland
Central to 17Z in Matabeleland South, while the VGR varied from 7? in
Mashonaland East to 0.7Z in Matabeleland South. The district of Murewa was
most affected, with a TGR of 78Z and a VGR of 24Z. Thus IDD can be said to
be a mild to moderate problems throughout the country, with some areas
having a severe endemnicity.
2.67        While the data on iron deficiency and anemia are spotty, there
is some evidence of a significant problem that is not being solved by
providing iron and folate tablets to pregnant women a-tending ante-natal
care clinics. A recent study of 1000 pregnant women visiting health
centers in Masvingo Province found that 50? had Hb levels below 10. Anemia
was also detected in 30? of the children aged 0-4 accompanying these women.
2.68        Vitamin A capsules (50,000 IU) are a part of the Zimbabwe
Essential Drugs Action Program, to be given to children with measles,
chronic diarrhea, and eye signs of vitamin A deficiency. However, it is
not known how often eye signs of vitamin A deficiency are seen, nor how
often these capsules are properly prescribed and taken for the indicated
diseases.
2.69        A review of the adult chronic disease problems frequently
related to diet has not been carried out for Zimbabwe. Many of these
diseases are becoming a cause of substantial morbidity and mortality in
Zimbabwe, including heart and circulatory diseases, cancers of the liver
and stomach, diabetes, chronic liver diseases and cirrhosis, ulcer of the
stomach and duodenum, and dental problems. Hypertension and dental
problems are among the top ten health problems among persons over five
years of age. Between 1980 and 1987 there was a 36? increase in the number
of deaths attributed to cancers of the liver and stomach, a 13? increase in
deaths from diseases of the circulatory system, a 35? increase in deaths



- 22 -
from diabetes, and a 43Z increase in deaths due to ulcers. In this same
period there was only a 1t increase in the number of deaths due to
nutritional deficiency and a decline in the number of deaths due to
infective and parasitic diseases and respiratory diseases. The role of
diet and of alcohol consumption in the etiology of many of these diseases
appears to merit some serious study and the development of related public
health programs. Zimbabwe has very little capability to include dietary
aspects in treatment. Few hospitals have dieticians -- even the central
teaching hospital in Harare is currently lacking one. An inadequate number
of posts for dieticians exist, but even nine of the existing ones are
unfilled. There is no in-country training for dieticians, and none are
being sent for training overseas.
Underlying Causes of Malnutrition
2.70        It does not appear that the persistent problem of failure to
grow in young children - and especially chronic undernutrition - is caused
in a major way by the disease episodes that account for malnutrition in
many other African countries. This is because Zimbabwe has made
substantial progress over the past decade in reducing the incidence of
early childhood disease, through its preventive health care programs.
Construction and staffing of rural health centers, expanded access to
improved latrines and water sources, programs for management of diarrhea
and acute respiratory infection, and increased vaccination coverage have
all helped to lower the incidence of a range of infectious diseases that
exacerbate malnutrition. The percentage of out-patient attendances for
diarrhea declined from 33Z in 1980 to less than lOX in 1988. One
additional factor that may contribute to lower incidence of diarrhea in
Zimbabwe, as compared to many other African countries, is the common use of
'mahewal and other fermented gruels for young child feeding. However, one
harmful practice found in Zimbabwe is to remove the infant immediately from
the mother's breapt. once diarrhea commences.
2.71        Food insecurity contributes to malnutrition in Zimbabwe,
especially chronic insecurity in communal farming areas with high
population density and high levels of male out-migration, as well as in
some commercial farming areas where wages are low and purchased food prices
are relatively high. Droughts resulted in transitory food insecurity in
the more arid parts of the country during the 1980s, although relief
efforts were highly effective in mitigating the nutritional impact.
2.72        Perhaps the most important factor leading to infant and child
malnutrition in Zimbabwe concerns child care and feedinR practices. This
is supported by the fact tLat the process of growth failure begins early in
the first year of life: an examination of the 1988 ZDHS data shows that the
process of becoming stunted begins before three months of age, a high
prevalence is reached by nine months, and peaks at 19 months of age.
Breastfeeding and complementation patterns are the main determinants of
growth at this vulnerable age, when infants are still somewhat protected
from disease and household food insecurity. Too few feeds per day with
foods low in caloric density during the weaning period are common probleris



- 23 -
among women overburdened with work and unable to afford special infant
foods or even much fat or sugar.
2.73        While the median duration of breastfeeding continues to be
about 20 months per child and only 10-15 of infants receive breast milk
for less than one year, several common practices in Zimbabwe tend to
mitigate the nutritSnnal benefits of breastfeeding. First, non-milk
supplements are communly given even to very young children: one study found
that among infants under three months of age, 26? in urban areas and 51? in
rural areas received such solid or semi-solid supplements. Such failure to
breast-feed exclusively increases the infants exposure to pathogens.
Second, the need to feed the young child frequently is apparently not
widely recognized: according to another study, three meals a day were
thought to be adequate by 44Z of mothers, and one or two meals daily by
13Z. Third, breastfeeding is sometimes stopped when the infant gets
diarrhea, thus aggravating dehydration and undernutrition: in a 1985
survey, 23Z of mothers stated that breastfeeding caused diarrhea if the
mother has sexual relations with her husband.
Nutrition Institutions and Programs
2.74        Thus far, the Ministry of Health has taken the lead in the
field of nutrition in Zimbabwe. As part of its primary health care
approach, the MOH changed its Dietetics Unit into a Nutrition Department
shortly after independence. The Department gained strength during the mid-
1980s, but in 1987 was incorporated into the Maternal and Child Health
Department as a Unit.
2.75        The MOH currently has posts for 24 nutritionists (six in
headquarters and two in each of the provinces), of which 12 are filled.
The large number of vacancies is due to a considerable degree to the
limited opportunities for advanced training available to MOH nutritionists:
in recent years, the Public Service Commission has only been allowing one
nutritionist to go abroad for study each year. There is also a high
vacancy rate for Instituional Domestic Supervisors who manage all food
services in hosiptals and other large instiutions. There are 61 nutrition
coordinators at district level (mainly to run the Community Food and
Nutrition Program - see below) and three dieticians, far from adequate
given the needs across the country. There are about 500 home demonstrators
(now community development workers) from the Ministry of Agriculture whose
extension responsibilities include nutrition-related tasks. The Ministry
of Education has become interested in recent years in the potential for
enhancing the learning capacity of students by investing in their health
and nutrition. The MOE has cooperated with the MOH Nutrition Unit in
planning a pilot project in conjunction with UNESCO, to be funded by SIDA.
2.76        One of Zimbabwe's most important nutrition initiatives has been
the Child Food and Nutrition Program (CFNP), which began in 1981 as a
drought relief effort aiming to reach the most vulnerable young children in
drought-prone area using locally-grown food and nutrition education. In
1984, over 250,000 children were reached by the program. Gradually, it
evolved into an intervention to deal with chronic malnutrition, by



- 24 -
mobilizing villagers to grow foods in a community gardens, rain-fed plots,
or poultry/rabbit projects, often linked to day care and special feeding
sessions for children identified as malnourished. There are currently over
2000 such CFNP projects, with 10-200 participants per project. Material
support (fencing materials, seeds, and fertilizer) is provided with funding
from SIDA. Technical back-up comes from Agritex, while MOH nutrition
coordinators help plan and manage activities at community level. At
national level, CFNP is run by an inter-sectorial steering commlttee
chaired by MOH, with similar committees at province, district, and ward
levels.
2.77        While the CFNP is generally regarded as having been successful
in combatting malnutrition, the program could still be improved through a
number of measures. These includet (a) greater targeting, especially to
large-scale commercial farms and to provinces/districts with relatively
high levels of malnutrition; (b) increased Government funding to ensure
sustainability; (c) improved reporting and monitoring and operations
research; (d) inclusion of community-based child growth monitoring as a
standard part of the program: (e) strengthened nutrition education and
training; (f) better integration of other ministries and their extension
workers, and stronger links with water and sanitation, functional literacy,
and pre-school activities; and (g) establishment of nutrition posts at
district level.
2.78        Growth monitoring has become well established in Zimbabwe at
hospitals and health centers, but not yet at community level despite
efforts to do so. Overall, the proportion of children surveyed in 1988 who
had no weights plotted on a growth chart varied from 7Z in Midlands
Province to 28Z in Manicaland. The average number of times that weights
were plotted during the first year of life ranged from 3.9 to 5.5 times in
the different provinces. In one district (Bubi, in Matabeleland North),
about 90Z of children under five are monitored at community level, and this
practice is spreading to other districts. Efforts are also under way to
use growth monitcring for promotion of better nutrition. A growth
monitoring training manual for community workers is currently at an
advanced stage of design. The MOH Nutrition Unit has long had plans to
review and strengthen the nutrition-related curricula of many extension
workers, but manpower constraints have largely prevented this from
happening.
2.79        Emphasis on nutrition education has always existed and the
population of Zimbabwe has continuously received messages from a number of
organizations over the past decade, although these have not been well-
coordinated.  Nutrition education has focused mainly on the need for a
balanced diet (food groups) and on child feeding messages such as the
importance of adding peanut butter to their food. Breastfeeding promotion,
including the WHO/UNICEF recommendation to practice exclusive breastfeeding
