for at least the first four months of life and to continue for two years,
has not figured prominently. The same is true of other information needed
by families to optimize care and feeding of infants and young children.
Furthermore, while the importance of a strong mass media component and of
social marketing in nutrition education was recognized at an early stage, a



- 25 -
shortage of local skills and technical assistance for this purpose have
hampered efforts in these areas.
2.80        A national iodine deficiency disease (IDD) program is beginning
to take shape, based largely on the iodation of salt. One major issue is
whether to iodate within Zimbabwe's borders all salt after import or to
pass a law requiring all imported salt to be iodated at the required level
(50 ppm) -- about 45 firms currently import salt. In either case, quality
control and monitoring of the iodine status of the population will need to
be maintained. There may also be a need to complement salt iodation with
the distribution of iodized oil capsules in remote high-prevalence areas of
the country. A pilot project and a cretinism survey, to be funded by SIDA,
are planned for 1991.
2.81        Finally, while pre-service nutrition training is not offered in
Zimbabwe, the MOH Nutrition Unit devotes a substantial amount of its time
to administering and teaching parts of the six-week Eastern, Central, and
Southern Africa in-service nutrition course, which has been held in Harare
for the past four years with financial and technical support from the
Netherlands.
2.82        The main donors to nutrition in Zimbabwe over the past decade
have been UNICEF and SIDA. UNICEF's contribution, amounting to about
US$0.1 million a year, has covered some training activities and the
purchase of child weighing scales and other equipment. SIDA funding,
averaging about US$0.5 million annually, has been allocated to a wide range
of items including basic support for the CPNP and overseas training in
nutrition. The recently-concluded three-year program (1990-92) of SIDA
assistance to health and nutrition amounts to about US$0.6 million, to be
spent on training, parts of the IDD control program, consolidation of the
CFNP, IEC on infant feeding, pilot nutrition surveillance at community
level, and support to develop a national food and nutrition policy.
D. World Bank Involvement in the PUN Sectors
2.83        A PHN Sector Survey (1983) provided the analytical basis for
the design of the Family Health Prolect (FHP1, Ln. 2744-ZIM, total cost
US$52.5 million), which became effective in early 1987 and is scheduled for
completion at the end of 1991.  Through the project, the Government has
endeavored to (a) expand family planning services by training MOH/MLGRUD
nurses and carrying out effective IEC activities; (b) strengthen the
management of health services at district level and in MOH headquarters by
training health administrators and other specialized staff; and (c) provide
improved rural health care by upgrading and equipping eight district
hospitals, about 60 rural health centers and 20 small rural hospitals.
During the implementation period for FHPl, the Bank and local officials
have collaborated on two major sector studies concerned with Population and
Family Planning Programs (1989) and Options for Financing Health Services
in Zimbabwe (1989).



- 26 -
2.84        Mid-term evaluations of FHP1 conducted by HOf and by a team of
donor officials in late 1989, as well as recent bi-annual reviews of
project performance by the Bank, have found that overall implementation has
been good. Total expenditures as of December 1990 amounted to Z$106
million or 115X of the estimated project costs. Disbursements have
generally been satisfactory, except for some training and IEC activities
where delays occurred in the early years. Project targets for facility
upgrading and family planning and management training have for the most
part been substantially achieved. The project is already beginning to have
a discernible positive impact on the quality of health and family planning
services offered in rural areas, and on capacity-building at district
level, especially through the training of district health teams, the
posting of district health administrators, and the provision of quality
housing for MOH staff.
2.85        As part of the preparation of FHP2, a comprehensive list of
lessons from the first project was drawn up and used to sharpen project
design. This list appears in Annex 7, Part 1. A few of the most important
lessons from the implementation experience in FHP1 that have been
incorporated in the design of FHP2 ares
(a)   While the first project has made an important contribution to
Zimbabwe's family planning efforts, a broader approach is required,
starting with a comprehensive national strategy and program and
leading to a major project component comprising expanded service
delivery, method mix diversification, manpower development,
management strengthening and IEC;
(b)   While the first project focused on the management of MOH training
programs, other critical management areas need to be addressed
urgently, especially financial management of the sector and the
management of medical equipment and supplies;
Cc)   Designs for upgrading of district hospitals and clinics need to be
more flexible and tailored to local factors (population, disease
patterns, existing facilities and communications). The designs
should also be made more cost-effective and fully take into account
the associated recurrent costs (staffing, operation and maintenance).
Construction activities should be staggered during the implementation
period, given the prevailing capacity constraints in the local
construction industry;
(d)   Project management systems need to be well established at the outset
of the project and strengthened systematically, through a combination
of adequate staffing in MOH, short courses and on-the-job training,
use of local consultants, and provision of office equipment and
supervisory vehicles. Special attention should be given to project
monitoring and reporting systems, budgeting and accounts, and
procurement; and
(e)   To expedite the implementation of large numbers of highly-
decentralized short training courses, operations research and IEC



- 27 -
activities and minor purchases of materials and equipment, a project
Special Account should be set up and effective procedures for
accounting and claiming reimbursements adopted. A separate Special
Account should be set up for the ZNFPC to facilitate timely
implementation of their family planning activities, especially since
ZNFPC has demonstrated its relatively strong financial management
capabilities.
2.86        The preparation of FHP2 was a broad-based, participatory
process emphasizing local Zimbabwean inputs, that started in May 1989 with
the formation of a Project Planning Committee (PPC). The committee, which
reported to the Secretary for Health, included representatives of a wide
range of MOH departments and provincial officials, as well the ZNFPC.  In
consultation with senior MOH staff, the PPC set planning guidelines,
including financial cost and staffing parameters and a preliminary outline
of project scope and composition, and requested a large number of proposals
from the various concerned MOH and ZNFPC units. Perhaps the most critical
planning decision was to limit the number of districts benefitting from
health infrastructure upgrading to the two neediest districts in each
province of the country.
2.87        During late 1989 and the first half of 1990, 24 provincial and
district proposals and an additional 10-12 MOH/ZNFPC headquarters proposals
were drafted and subsequently reviewed by the PPC and World Bank/donor
missions. On the basis of these reviews, the various proposals were
revised and costed, and in some cases augmented by consultants engaged by
the Government and Bank with financing from a special Japanese Grant
Facility. During the second half of 1990, ZNFPC prepared a National Five-
Year Family Planning Strategy and Implementation Plan (summarized in Annex
2, Part 2), incorporating existing program and project plans by USAID and
UNFPA, which was used for preparation of six proposals for the FP
component. Following pre-appraisal in September 1990, a team of local
planning consultants supervised by the PPC was assigned the task u.
polishing the large set of project proposals and drafting a summary volume.
A donor mission consisting of Bank staff and representatives of a number of
interested donors (DANIDA, Netherlands, NORAD, and ODA) appraised FHP2 in
November-December 1990.
2.88        Officials of the Ministry of Finance, Economic Development, and
Planning (MFEPD) also played an important role ir the preparation of FHP2,
reviewing the project at several stages and ensuring that the project was
consistent with national planning guidelines and development policies. In
this regard, MOH endeavored to make FHP2 the centerpiece of the investment
program for health, population, and nutrition during the period of the
five-year plan (1991-95). MOH officials responsible for elaboration of the
strategic plan for the health sector coordinated the preparation of FHP2,
too, to ensure consistency between the two exercises. During the second
half of 1990, ZNFPC prepared a National Five-Year Family Planning Strategy
and Implementation Plan (shown in draft in Annex 2:C), incorporating
existing program and project plans by USAID and UNFPA, which was the basis
for the proposed FP component, and ensures that the project will contribute



- 28 -
to a well thought out broad strategy for improving and expanding family
planning services in Zimbabwe.
2.89        Finally, the project has been designed to support the
Government's impending structural adjustment Program in several respects.
Investments are targeted to the most underserved rural districts of
Zimbabwe, and to low-income households in those districts who will need to
be protected from any adverse social impacts of the adjustment process.
The project includes support for setting up a nutritional surveillance
system which will monitor the impact of the adjustment program and enable
timely action to be taken if necessary. The incremental recurrent costs
associated with the project have been minimized by emphasizing
rehabilitation of existing facilities and redeployment of staff (especially
from urban to rural areas). Furthermore, by assisting improvements in MOH
planning, budget and accounting procedures and an increase ir. cost recovery
through adjusted fees and strengthened billing and collection performance,
the project should actually lead to a substantial rise in direct charges
for health and family planning services, to supplement allocations from
general Government revenues.



- 29 -
III. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
3.1         The broad objectives of FHP2 are tot
(a)   improve maternal and child health and nutrition status;
(b)   reduce the rate of population growths
(c)   ensure that households in the target distr.cts (especially low-
income families) have access to basic health, population, and
nutrition services;
(d)   increase the output of high-quality trained Zimbabwean health
workers (especially in family planning, midwifery, nutrition, and
a wide range of management skills), in the context of improved
policies and practices for training and personnel management;
(e)   enhance MOH, ZNFPC, and relevant NGO capacity to plan and manage
health, population, and nutrition activities, at national,
provincial, district, community, and facility levels; and
(f)   adopt measures, systems, and skills to increase allocative and
technical efficiency and mobilize significant additional resources
for health, nutrition, and population.
3.2         To monitor the effectiveness of project activities, the
Government has developed a series of health/population/nutritic status,
service delivery, and other process indicators. On a nationwide basis,
the key indicators includes
(a)   lowering the infant mortality rate from 53 in 1990 to 43 in 1996;
(b)   increasing the percentage of children receiving the complete
vaccination course, at the correct age/interval, from 702 in 1990
to 85Z in 1996;
(c)   reducing the share of children aged 6-36 months who are
malnourished (less than 80 of the reference weight for age) from
12Z in 1990 to 52 in 1996;
'd)   increasing the percentage of pregnant women receiving antenatal
care from 902 in 1990 to 95Z by 1996 and increasing the percentage
who deliver in a health facility 70Z to 852;
(e)   lowering the total fertility rate from 5.5 in 1986 to 4.5 in 1996,
and raising the percentage of married women of reproductive age
using modern methods of contraception from 362 to 48Z;
(f)   increasing the percentage of married women using permanent and
semi-permanent methods from 42 in 1988 to 122 in 1996;



- 30 -
(g)   training 3,500 nurses and 120 doctors in family planning;
(h)   increasing the proportion of nurses in district and rural service
trained in midwifery to 602 by 1996;
(i)   increasing the level of cost recovery by MOH from 3? of recurrent
budget expenditures in 1990 to at least 102 by July 1, 1995.
Government's annual target increases will be monitored during
implementation.
For each of the project objectives and related components/subcomponents,
a more detailed set of progress indicators has been elaborated. In many
cases, the provincial and district health planning teams have developed
their own targets.
3.3         The scope of FHP2 is national in a number of program areas
where country-wide coverage is both vital to achieve a meaningful
health/population/nutrition impact and is also financially affordables
these include family planning, improved maternal health and child
nutrition, midwifery training, and health management strengthening. On
the other hand, the Government has decided to concentrate project
resources for upgrading rural health infrastructure and provision of
related equipment, transport, and radio communications in 16 of
Zimbabwe's 55 districts. These 16 target districts, which were selected
using a series of criteria to define "need" (e.g., disease incidence,
current level of service delivery, number of health personnel and
hospital beds in relation to the population, etc.), are:
-     Mudzi and Murehwa (Mashonaland East)
-     Rushinga and Guruve (Mashonaland Central)
-     Kadoma and Kariba (Mashonaland West)
-     Bulalimamangwe and Kezi-Matobo (Matabeleland South)
-     Bubi and Nkayi (Matabeleland North)
-     Buhera and Mutasa (Manicaland)
-     Chibi and Mwenezi (Masvingo)
-     Chivhu and Kwekwe (Midlands)
A profile of these 16 districts is given in Annex 4, Part 1.
3.4         In broad outline, the six main project components and
subcomponents, to be implemented during 1992-96, are:
(a)   Support to Family Planning: training, outreach services, clinic
services, special youth services, IEC, and management support;
(b)   Maternal and Child Health Services: midwifery training, maternity
equipment, MCH/FP in-service training, provision of transport and
radio communications links between health centers and hospitals, a
school health aiid nutrition program, and operations research and
program monitoring and evaluationt
(c)   Nutrition: community-based growth monitoring, nutrition gardens,
surveillance, research, promotion of breastfeeding, combatting
micro-nutrient deficiencies, nutrition training, and IEC;



- 31 -
(d)   Strengthening Rural Health Delivea: selective upgrading and
equipping of district hospitals and rural health centers,
construction of staff housing;
(e)   Healcth                       i zn-     Developantt institutional strengthening at
central, provincial, and district levels, systems development
including decentralising the personnel function, and providing
modest district trainlng facilitW.es; and
(f)   Management Strengthening: training, systems development, related
hardware, and limited technical assistance to build critical
capacity in financial management, materials and equipment
management, district-level health services management, and sector
planning.
B. Detailed Project Description
Family Planning (base cost US$15.7 million)
3.5         In line with the national FP strategy (see Annex 2, Part 2),
the project will aim to (a) increase access to high-quality FP carvices
and (b) expand use of longer-term methods. Current and projected levels
of family planning use, by contraceptive method, are given in Annex 2,
Part 3. Two subcomponents correspond to these aims. Two complementary
sub-components to facilitate their achievement are (c) provision of
appropriate IEC and (d) strengthening of FP program management.
operationally, the project budget for PP activities is divided into six
parts corresponding to the implementing ZNFPC Units, namely Training,
IEC, YAS, Clinic-based Services (Medical/Clinical), Outreach (CBD), and
Management (Executive Director's office).
3.6         Increasing Access to FP Services  Following the national FP
strategy, and taking account of parallel complementary activities by
other donors, this subcomponent will concentrate support to the
expansion and upgrading of two types of FP services in Zimbabwe, namely
those delivered through fixed health or FP facilities (Government or
ZNFPC) and through rural outreach networks. In addition, the
subcomponent will help strengthen FP training irstitutions to meet
additional demand arising from the much expanded volume of training that
will be necessary.
3.7         The improve: int of service delivery at Government fixed
facilities under FHP2 is in large part a continuation of the PP training
component of FHP1. It will provide for: FP in-service training for
government (MOH, MOLG or City Health Department) nurses not covered
under FHP1; refresher training for government nurses; and training of FP
trainers for City Health training centers, for existing vacancies in the
eight district MCH/FP training centers set up under FHPI and for the 16
centers to be established under the project. Strengthening of ZNFPC
training capacity to handle the increased intakes under both FHP2 and
the parallel UNFPA and USAID programs will also be financed.



- 32 -
3.8         Specifically, the training element of this subcomponent will
finance training fees, transport and per diem costs for the following
courses and trainee follow-up: (a) training of trainer courses for
MCH/FP trainers; (b) follow-up visits to new FHP2 district trainers; (c)
FP clinical courses for nurses at district MCH/FP training centers,
ZNFPC training centers and City Health training centers; (d) bi-annual
FP refresher courses for nurses; and (e) annual Contraceptive Technology
Update courses for MCH/FP and City Health trainers. To accommodate the
planned expansion of both teaching establishment and training workload,
funds will also be provided for: (a) construction of additional
facilities (classrooms and offices) at the ZNFPC training centers in
Harare and Bulawayo; (b) provision of additional training equipment and
transport for both centers; and (c) short-term training fellowships for
ZNFPC tutors in training management and specialist training areas.
3.9         In addition to these training activities, this subcomponent
will provide funds for expansion and upgrading of the ZNFPC fixed clinic
network. Three provincial Youth Advisory Centers, modelled on the
existing center in Harare, will also be established to complement five
centers being constructed with USAID support, making a total of one
cek.ter in every provincial capital.
3.10        To improve access to rural outreach FP services, the project
will aid the expansion and revitalization of the current CBD-based
outreach system (shown in Map No. 22482) through placing additional CBDs
in currently unserved or underserved areas, improving transport for CBDs
and establishitg alternative or supplementary community-based
contraceptive delivery systems. Full final specification of this latter
activity will have to await the results of the ZNFPC's forthcom3ng
operational research on covmunity-based depot-holder (CDH) systems,
which is scheduled to begin field work this year and produce preliminary
results next year. However, it has been budgeted for the moment on the
assumption that a network of 2,800 CDHs will be established over the
life of the project. Assurances were given by the Government that ZNFPC
will recruit and deploy at least 80 additional CBDs prior to June 30,
1993. Subsequent decisions on recruitment of CBDs wrill be based on the
results of ZNFPC studies of family planning manpower needs and options
for rural outreach services, to be completed by December 31, 1991 and
December 31, 1992, respectively. Supplementary outreach will be
provided by one mobile clinic attached to each of sdven of the eight
ZNFPC provincial offices (excluding Manicaland which is already
receiving a new replacement mobile under an IPPF grant). The new mobile
clinics, like those already in use, will provide FP clinic services and
back-up for CBDs in areas without access to fixed health or ZNFPC
facilities or hence CBDs (see Map No. 22840 for their planned venues).
3.11        Increasina Use of Longer-Term Methods  This subcomponent
builds on and continues development from the Urban Family Planning
Component of FHP1. It would support further expansion and development
of a network of (mostly urban) PP service centers capable of providing
IUD, sterilization and NORPLANT services (the latter being mainly
supported, however, by the NORPLANT component of the USAID program). A
major element will be the establishment of an additional 25 VSC/IUD
centers within existing government/ZNFPC facilities. These would



- 33 -
include 16 HOH hospitals (eight provincial, eight district), slx ZNFPC
clinics and three facilities in Harare, Bulawayo and Chitungwiza.
Additional staff, training and equipment will also be provided to
upgrade services in other facilities. The development of FP service
policies, protocols, quality indicators and monitoring and evaluation
will be supported.
3.12         Specifically, the project will finance the following: (a)
upgrading of facilities and provision of equipment at the 25 centers to
provide adequate premises for IUD and VSC service delivery; (b)
evaluation of the current FP Clinical and IUD training courses; (c)
local costs of training of IUD/STD trainers from ZNPPC, HOH provincial
teams and City Health; (d) IUD/STD training of MCH/FP trainers and
ZNFPC clinic and Central Hospital staff; (e) training of MOH and City
Health nurses in IUDISTD services; (f) training of doctor/nurse teams in
VSC and of doctors in IUD/STD services; (g) training of VSC counsellors
to cover all VSC sites, Central and Provincial hospitals and City Health
Departments; (h) training of doctors and nurses in NORPLANT services;
and (i) development and production of policies, protocols and quality-of
service indicators for FP services covering all methode.
3.13        Strengthening of PP Program Management   This subcomponent
will support the increasingly heavy task of managing the expanding FP
program by strengthening ZNFPC administrative and managerial
capabilities. This will include review and appropriate reinforcement of
ZNFPC central management, planning and administration; computerizing the
financial administration and accounting and the MIS and management
systems in all ZNFPC provincial offices; and enlarging ZNFPC provincial
office establishments. (See Annex 2, Part 1, for organigrams of ZNFPC
headquarters and provincial offices and a list of added posts.)
3.14        Specifically, the subcomponent will finance at central
level: (a) a full management review of the ZNFPC, plus appropriate
follow-up; (b) short-term local technical assistance to implement the
national FP strategy through preparation of provincial and district
strategiee and the first annual workplans at national, provincial and
district levels; (c) annual workshops for provincial and district
HOH/ZNFPC MCH/FP managers for program review/planning/supervision; (d) a
review of current planning procedures and of the contraceptive
commodities management system; and (e) stren6thening of the central
tachnical units' capacity to manage and supervise through additional
training, transport and staff.
3.15         At provincial level, the project will finance:
computerization of financial administration and accounting and the MIS;
(b) establishment of eight provincial IEC officers and 32 additional YAS
officers (in order to bring the total YAS workforce up to 55, or one
officer per district).
3.16        IEC  This subcomponent will provide both general and
specific IEC support to the objectives and activities described above.
It includes IEC materials and activities for (a) family planning and YAS
trainers, service providers and clients; (b) general community education
and outreach; and (c) education of leaders and influential persons.



- 34 -
Activities under this subcomponent will be enhanced by strengthening of
provincial-level IEC management (para. 3.14) and of the ZNFPC/MOH audio-
visual (AV) unit set up under FHP1.
3.17        Support to IEC for training and service providers will
improve their knowledge and communication skills, hence the quality and
range of information and services effectively available to clients, by:
(a) developing and producing instructional and promotional materials for
the use of FP trainers, service providers and clients,.including IEC
kits for trainers and service providers and IEC materials and equipment
for the 25 new IUD/VSC service centers; (b) monitoring and conducting
research on FP client reactions and attitudes, through surveys and
analysis of service statistics; (c) carrying out a range of activities
aimed at improving knowledge and attitudes of senior medical personnel,
including an FP Method Mix Symposium; and (d) developing improved IEC
materials and activities for the YAS program, including the new YAS
centers.
3.18        Support to IEC for general community education and outreach
is designed to assist the rural outreach network by disseminating and
improving knowledge and understanding of the rationale and methods of
family planning in rural communities. This type of IEC, which will be
particularly dependent on the work of the new provincial IEC officers,
will includes (a) reprinting materials (including some developed under
FHP1) for use in the training and work of community-based workers; and
(b) developing community-related promotional materials and activities
(e.g., rural newspaper articles, motivational talks, workshops for
community motivation teams, establishment of a community prize fund for
the rural community closest to MCH/FP targets, etc).
3.19        Support to IEC for influential people and opinion leaders
will give general assistance to the entire family planning component by
disseminating and improving knowledge and understanding of the rationale
and methods of family planning among influential persons. To do this,
ZNFPC will organize national, provincial and district-level conferences
and workshops for Parliamentarians and influential persons, as well as
workshops for media personnel. Suitable promotional materials for these
groups will also be developed and produced. A wide range of promotional
materials for the mass media (radio, TV, film, video, newspapers, etc)
will also be funded, in order to continue and reinforce ZNFPC's efforts
to mobilise general public opinion in favour of family planning, child
spacing and smaller families.
Maternal and Child Health (base cost US$10.1 million)
3.20        The key objectives of the maternal and child health program
for the next five years ares
-     to reduce infant (especially perinatal) and child mortality,
focussing particularly on the top three causes of death (AMI,
diarrhea and valnutrition);



- 35 -
-     to reduce maternal mortality and morbidity, work towards
eliminating maternal deaths due to preventable causes, and get
reliable estimates of maternal mortality.
The project would address several priority areas to help achieve the
service delivery and morbidity and mortality reduction targetst
improving coverage of antenatal, delivery and postnatal services,
ensuring that facilities are properly equipped and staff adequately
trained to provide optimum care 'o mothers and newborns, better
management of cases, improving communication and transport links for
patients being referred to higher levels of the system, improving and
expanding nutrition programs, and providing better health education to
parents and children.
3.21        Equipment  Inadequate equipment for &ntenatal care,
deliveries, and neonates has compromised the quality of care, especially
in rural facilities. The equipment provided by SIDA, UNICEF and WHO has
met some critical needs, but more is required. The MCH DeE rtment is
preparing standard equipment lists for facilities at various levels and
situations (e.g. rural health centers with/without electricity) and an
inventory system is being developed that will be easy to update and that
will include repair records. The project would provide all facilities
in the 16 project districts with the standard equipment (see Annex 3 for
main items and costs). In-service training will be carried out to
ensure that staff are well trained in proper use and care of equipment.
3.22        To ensure that the emergency referral and routine
supervisory systems function properly in the target districts, a total
of 24 ambulances and 16 four-wheel drive supervision vehicles will be
purchased under the project. In order to improve the versatility of the
supervision vehicles and to facilitate pharmaceutical distribution, 16
trailers will be included. A boat will be provided for Kariba District,
to be used for emergencies and for routine supply and supervision
purposes. About 80 motorcycles will be purchased for distribution to
outreach workers in the target districts. Point-to-point radio
communication equipment will be installed in RHCs that do not currently
have telephones, to enable RHC personnel to call for emergency services
and consult with their district referral hospitals. A detailed
inventory of existing communications, as well as preparation of detailed
radio equipment lists, budget estimates, and implementation plans will
be carried out in the first half of 1991.
3.23        MCH/FP Training, Including Refresher Courses  The project
would continue to support the training of nurses (SCNs and SRNs) at
district level to provide a full range of family planning and HCH
services in an integrated way. The project aim would be to train a
further 4,600 nurses in the 6 week MCH/FP course, which is being
successfully administered in the eight districts of the first project
(budgeted under family planning training subcomponent). ZNFPC would
train 32 (i.e. two per district) nurse-tutors from the district
hospitals as master-trainers (see para 3.8). Funds would be provided
for these trainers to conduct three MCH/FP courses each year in each
district, using rented premises until the in-service training facilities
at the district hospitals being upgraded are completed. The funds would



- 36 -
cover transport, subsistence, rental of premises where necessary and
materials for training. In addition, the project would provide for an
additional 3 one-week training courses each year for 15 participants in
each district, for refresher courses in areas of need to be identified
during project implementation.
3.24        School Health and Nutrition Program  The school health and
nutrition program presently has very low coverage outside of the main
urban areas, and has been hampered by staff shortages and inadequate
transport. The MCH Department aims to reach 802 of primary school
children in the 16 project districts. The project will provide support
for training at least one teacher in each school to do basic weighing,
measuring height, and screening for hearing and vision defects. These
activities could be incorporated into lessons on weights and measures
and health. Nurses would visit all schools annually to conduct hearing
and eye tests on children identified during screening, and to identify
children with other health or nutrition problems, to refer them for
appropriate treatment or further tests. The project would provide
audiometers, scales and height meters, and first aid kits for all
schools. (The kits could be replenished as necessary at local clinics,
and would include chloroquine in malarial areas.) The project would
also provide funds to print 500,000 child health cards.
3.25        Reeearch Studies  Existing statistics on maternal mortality
only cover the 702 of deliveries that take place in facilities, and the
vital registration data are incomplete. There is therefore no reliable
estimate of the maternal mortality rate, trends cannot be charted, and
not enough is known confidently to target efforts to achieve the biggest
reduction in maternal mortality. The project includes a community-based
study in selected areas which should enable an accurate estimate to be
made of maternal mortality rates (see Annex 3, part 8). The study will
be carried out in conjunction with the Zimbabwe Medical School staff,
and will complement ongoing research into the causes of 100 recent
maternal deaths. The project would also fund research into the
effectiveness and usefulness of selected in-service training courses, to
enable better training to be designed and delivered.
3.26        Monitoring and Evaluation  A comprehensive survey of health
and nutrition services and immunization coverage was carried out in 1988
and will be repeated in 1991 under the first Family Health Project. The
mid-term review of the proposed second project would include a similar
survey in 1994 to enable continued monitoring of progress. Assurances
were given b; Government that these surveys would be undertaken in 1991
and 1994 and the results submitted to the World Bank by March of each
following year. The project would also provide computers and training
in computer use, data entry, tabulation and simple analysis to district
staff, so that the data can be analyzed and incorporated in district
level planning (as well as being aggregated nationally). Procurement of
the computers and training for users would be phased over 3 years. (The
computtrs would also be used for general management and budgeting and
for the decentralized personnel information system, see para 3.46).
3.27        Health Education  The MOH Health Education Unit (HEU) and
field health education officers will be trained in research, social



- 37 -
marketing, planning and program management, conducted during periodic
visits (totalling 4 months) by a communications specialist/trainer who
will also advise the HEU and help plan (see Annex 3 for TOR). The
project would include funds to train nurses in communication skills, and
for studies of knowledge, attitude and practice (KAP), materials
development and testing, printing/production and distribution, mass
media messages (i.e. TV and cinema, radio, newspaper, billboards and
folk drama), and for evaluation of IEC campaigns. The project would
fund 8 well-equipped mobile communications vans and additional equipment
(projectors, videos, cameras and megaphones) for project districts.
3.28        Midwifery Training  Th, )verall goal of this sub-component
is to contribute to improved maternity and infant care by relieving the
shortage of maternity-trained nurses, especially in rural facilities,
strengthening basic and post-basic training for nurses, midwives, and
community nurses, supporting the community attachment which is an
important part of basic nurse training, and improving the skills of
practicing midwives through better supervision and in-service training.
3.29        The project would help to increase the number of midwifery
tutors to 32 by 1996 by supporting a doubled intake of students to the
2-year University-level Diploma of Nursing Education (DNE) (every year
instead of every second year) and creating a midwifery track in the DNE.
The Department of Nursing Science faculty would be strengthened by
getting an interim temporary faculty member for 16 months, and provision
made for (rented) accommodation for the additional midwifery-track DNE
students. Midwifery technical and teaching update modules would be
incorporated in the DNE and in the Health Teachers Diploma (HTD), which
prepares clinical instructors who teach the midwifery course for SCNs in
mission and Government institutions. (It is also intended to increase
the intake to the HTD, funded by the German Foundation.) Clinical
instructors and midwives who supervise DNE students during practicals
would receive training to update their clinical skills and improve
supervisory and teaching skills.
3.30        To enable an increase in the percent of nurses trained in
midwifery to 502 overall and 602 in rural health centers and hospitals,
the project would expand the capacity of eight existing nursing schools
by 100 places in all (extra classrooms and dormitories, and mini-buses).
An advisor will assist the Nursing Directorate to develop plans and
procedures to improve student selection and deployment; the project will
also support their implementation.
3.31        Basic and in-service nursing training would be strengthened
to help improve the quality of MCH and maternity care provided by nurses
and midwives. The temporary UZ Nursing Department faculty member would
help the Nursing Directorate review the basic nursing curriculum and
practical training experience, especially the obstetric/MCH/FP parts.
The community health secondment for SRNs would be improved by holding
training workshops for those who supervise and guide the students,
providing vehicles for supervision visits and the field costs of
supervisory visits to students by tutors, and ensuring that 32 of the
new/upgraded RHCs in project districts have reasonable accommodation for
two students and simple cooking facilities. Two week up-date courses in



- 38 -
midwifery would be given to 2,080 midwives during the project; and 220
DNOs, CNOs, and matrons would attend a series of 5-day course in health
service and staff management, to be designed jointly by the Zimbabwean
Institute of Management, local consultants and experienced health
service managers. Many staff in rural health centers have not had much
opportunity for in-service training. The Nursing Directorate intends to
develop a pilot distance learning program in MCH/midwifery, family
planning and young child nutrition, and test its effectiveness in
providing continuing education for 250 nurses in rural areas. It is
expected to be much less costly than traditional in-service courses. A
coordinator would be hired for three years, would go to Kenya on a study
tour to learn about the AMREF program, assess needs in Zimbabwe, and
manage the development and production of learning modules, and the
supervision and follow-up of participating students. The program would
be carefully evaluated after three years. The project would also
support a study to determine how many ex-combatant SCNs do not have the
educational attainment necessary for the SCMN course, and the
advantages, problems and options for offering a special program to
enable this cadre to become certified maternity nurses. Funds would be
set aside to design a course if recommended by the study.
3.32        This program will be a substantial challenge for the Nursing
Directorate and the Department of Nursing Sciences at the University,
which also need to make and implement important decisions and plans
concerning the proposed unification of the SCNs and SRNs into a single
nursing cadre, staff an expanding health service and improve the quality
of nursing care with constrained resources and staffing increases, and
reduce the high attrition rate of qualified nurses and tutors. The
project would provide funds for staff devAlopment through training on-
the-job and/or abroad, and a word processor, printer and xerox machine
for the Directorate.
Nutrition (base cost US$ 3.4 million)
3.33        This component would expand and strengthen nutrition
activities in the 16 project districts in response to their requests and
introduce innovative targeted programs, described in para 3.35-3.36
below. Priority would be given to districts with severe nutrition
problems and which showed particular interest and commitment. The main
problem to be tackled is persistent stunting, which still affects about
30Z of under-five children, and in which infant feeding practices seem
to play an important role.
3.34        Implementation of the proposed nutrition activities will
depend on the availability of adequately trained manpower. The
Nutrition Unit currently has only four staff at central level, down from
six a year ago. The project would contract a consultant nutritionist
for the first two years of the project to advise and assist the
Nutrition Unit in running local training courses and coordinating new
programs, and to provide on-the-job training (see Annex 3, part 10 for
terms of reference). It is expected that additional new staff will be
recruited nationally during the project for headquarters, and, as
recommended by the 1989 National MCH consultative meeting, 8 community



- 39 _
nurses or environmental technicians will be recruited and trained as
district nutrition workers to test the advantages of a cadre of
nutrition staff at this level.
3.35        The project would strengthen and improve targeting of the
Community Food and Nutrition Programme (CFNP), and link it to community-
based growth monitoring (CBGM) of all chilaren (not just those seen in
facilities) in communities most at risk. Project support would be
targeted to (a) workers on large-scale commercial farms; (b) new
res'.ctlement areas; (c) areas provided with improved water sources
intended to be linked to community gardens; and (d) communities with
more than 10? of under-fives "below the line" according to community-
based growth monitoring data or more than 20Z of under-fives 'below the
line" according to growth monitoring data from the nearest health
center. (The criterion is lower for communities which have proved their
commitment to nutrition by implementing community-based growth
monitoring, whose data would tend to be more reliable.) The project
would support pyramidal orientation, training and planning workshops
(from national down to ward and community-level) for the new community-
based program, and would train 40 additional nutrition coordinators to
be recruited over the first two years of the project, to manage the
expanded program. Assurances were given by Government that these 40
additional staff will be recruited and deployed prior to June 30, 1993.
Garden inputs: Participating communities would be given the minimum
necessary fencing, seed or fertilizer, and hardy fruit trees such as
guava, papaya and mango, to provide vitamin A and vitamin C. Advice
would be sought from Agritex on trees most likely to thrive in
particular areas. Wood lots would be added in areas where women have to
walk long distances to obtain fuel, to reduce their time and energy
constraints. The project would support pilot activities aimed at
improving preservation, storage and processing of the foods produced in
one district starting in 1993. Study tours for groups of leaders of new
project areas to places where successful projects are already under way
will speed up the learning process for communities, especially regarding
CBGM. Scales and growth-monitoring cards would be provided to
communities, and funds set aside for baseline surveys, a midterm review,
and program evaluation in 1996. Transport: To enable successful
implementation of the expanded programs, the project would provide a 4-
wheel drive supervision vehicle for each of the eight provincial
nutrition teams; nutrition coordinators would be provided with bicycles
or small motorcycles depending on area to be covered, and two seven-ton
trucks would be purchased for hauling the project inputs, especially
bulky fencing (commercial transport companies refuse to deliver in rural
areas).
3.36        Information, education and communication (IEC) activities to
support the CBGM and CFNP would brief communities on details of the
program and how to participate, young child feeding and the importance
of fresh fruits as a source of vitamin A and of vitamin C which enhances
absorption of iron when fruit is eaten after meals. The project would
fund the development, testing and production of a nutrition kit for use
by Village Community Workers, and training in their use for trainers,
district and ward nutrition teams, other health staff and extension
workers, and VCWs. The kit would consist of a tough carrying case, a



- 40 -
scale and growth charts, copies of a colorful laminated single-page
leaflet to be distributed to the community, with a few key messages in
Shona on one side and Ndebele on the other, a loose-leaf handbook with
information to answer auestions commonly asked by the public on
nutrition issues, an A5 size flip chart of key messages and graphics, a
small plastic bag of iodized salt and a small inexpensive kit (made in
India) for testing whether salt contains adequate '-vels of iodine.
This will be used for explaining to families the importance of iodized
salt, and for testing their salt.
3.37        A second major nutrition goal will be to increase the very
low prevalence of women in Zimbabwe who breast feed exclusively for A-6
months, as part of efforts to improve infant feeding practices. The
recent WHO/UNICEF recommendations for integrated national campaigns will
be adapted to Zimbabwe, with an emphasis on health worker traininig in
lactation management and support to working women.  The project 'ill
fund training in lactation management (which is not included in existing
health worker training) for nutrition and nursing staff. A baseline
survey of hospital practices throughout Zimbabwe would be conducted and
a workshop held for relevant staff from all central and provincial
hospitals. Periodic monitoring wouild track progress toward the target
of 1002 of hospitals conforming with the WHO/UNICEF '10 Steps' by 1996
(see Annex 3). The project would support activities through which the
Nutrition Unit could work with responsible authorities and interested
NGOs to review and improve (if necessary), monitor and enforce the
Zimbabwe Code of Marketing for Breast-milk Substitutes. The project
would also provide funds for IEC activities to be carried out in
conjunction with the health education unit: a total of 4-5 months (in
short visits as needed during the project) of a communication/social
marketing expert 1:o guide and train in IEC/social marketing techniques;
research into beliefs, and breastfeeding practices (including of health
workers and school-teachers who tend to be very influetitial especially
for young mothers); development and testing of appropriate messages and
materials, mass media message delivery, and evaluation of the campaign.
Improved breastfeeding practices could also improve contraceptive
protection for women not using other methods.
3.38        As part of the program to improve infant nutrition, the
project would also fund research to facilitate more effective
interventions for diarrhea management and infant feeding, and which
would guide the development of IEC messages and materials. The MCH
Department would seek research collaborators at Zimbabwe University and
other local and international institutions. There are two sets of
research questionst
(a)   How does infant growth respond when mothers are advised to
practice exclusive breastfeeding for 4-6 months? What types of
complementary foods best meet various needs in different settings,
based on local, inexpensive and time-saving foods? What is the
potential for promoting germination (to increase energy density),
fermentation (to reduce the effects of contamination), and
village-level production of toasted cereal-legume mixtures as
time-saving 'convenience foods'?



- 41 -
(b)   How widespread and strong are beliefs that breastfeeding causes
diarrhoea? Are there any messages that would be effective in
convincing most women to continue breastfeeding (and feeding other
foods) during diarrhoea? Are there any fluids commonly available
in homes which could be given to children as soon as they get
diarrhoea in order to prevent dehydration? Are health workers
aware of these issues?
3.39        To help control iodine deficiency disorders, the project
will establish a national monitoring system for iodated salt (which is
just beginning to enter the country in an uncontrolled way), and urinary
iodine and TSH hormone levels in sentinel centers in one high-risk
district in each province, by providing equipment and supplies for
testing, training of four laboratory technicians and their salaries on a
declining basis. These inputs would complement the training for the IDD
program and pilot capsule distribution in one district being funded by
SIDA, and which is expected to be extended to all the affected areas of
the country.
3.40        The nutrition unit has been asked to help monitor the impact
of Government's structural adjustment program on vulnerable people in
Zimbabwe. The project will assist by funding short-term technical
assistance (hired locally where possible) to help select sentinel
surveillance sites, develop a nutritional surveillance system and train
staff; a computer; and baseline survey and the costs of collecting,
analysing and compiling the data regularly and rapidly.
3.41         Institutional Domestic Supervisors manage food services in
hospitals and other institutions. The present annual output of 10 do
not meet demand, and many posts are vacant. The project will support an
expanded intake to the 1 year course by providing teaching equipment and
supplies (kitchen ware and appliances, food supplies, books, overhead
projector); student allowances and the transport costs for taking
students to sites for practical work (using the minibuses budgeted under
the midwifery component).
Strengthening Rural Health Delivery (base cost US$61.0 million)
3.42        To improve access to, and the quality of, rural health,
nutrition, and family planning services in the 16 target districts, a
package of rural health facility upgrading, transport, communications,
and related staffing and staff development will be implemented in each
district under the project. In line with established MOH norms, the aim
will be to ensure that each family has access to a rural health center
(RHC) within eight kilometers, offering the full range of preventive
(vaccinations, growth monitoring, antenatal, etc.) and outpatient
curative care, equipped to perform uncomplicated deliveries, and capable
of supporting and supervising community-based workers and programs. In
addition, by the end of the project period, each of the 16 target
districts should have at least one good-quality district hospital able
to provide the full range of first-line referral services, including
basic surgery, treatment of most accidents and illnesses, and care for
premature births.



- 4L -
3.43        The specific contents of the investment package will vary
from district to district, in line with their detailed needs as
identified during project appraisal, bearing in mind population,
catchment area, existing facilities, etc. - there is no standard
"blueprint" approach to this component.
3.44        As stated above, the project will cater for a district
hospital in each district. In a few cases where no hospital exists
(e.g. Mudzi, UMP, Kezi-Matobo, Mwenezi), a new complex will be
constructed. In the rest of the districts, an existing hospital will be
upgraded.  While most of the project hospitals belong to IOH. in two
districts (Guruve and Buhera), a church mission hospital designated by
the Government to serve as the district referral facility will benefit
from the upgrading. Typically, the district facility will include: an
outpatient block; a materna_ and child health unit; maternity, male,
female, and pediatric wards; kitchen, laundry, and administrative
offices; mortuary and incinerator; a small teaching wing; and staff
housing.
3.45        MOH has carried out an extensive review of the designs for
hospitals and RHCs built under FHP1 in order to get better value for the
new capital investments and reduce associated recurrent costs. As a
result, the preliminary designs for the FHP2 hospitals are superior to
the eight FHP1 district hospitals in several respects: (a) the hospitals
are smaller and more flexible in their design, with bed capacity linked
closely to the population size and other nearby hospital facilities; (b)
space for patient care and ancillary services has been kept to a minimum
consistent with good quality care; and (c) the mix of staff housing has
been modified to include a larger number of low-cost flatlets and bed-
sitters which are preferred by MOH staff. The average cost of an
upgraded hospital in the proposed project is about Z$6.5 million, as
compared to a cost of about Z$14 million for the district hospitals in
FHP1 (1990 prices). Since facilities are being designed to meet the
particular circumstances and conditions at different locations, rather
than using the "full-size" standard stock design of FHP1, size varies
from 54-120 beds, with most in the 78-104 bed range. Some will be small
satellite hospitals, providing basic services to populations located at
a great distance from the main district hospital and referring more
complicated cases to the district facility. When combined with a
staggered schedule of construction (para 5.12), this should ensure
timely and cost-effective implementation. MOH will send to the World
Bank for review the final designs for each district hospital, before
proceeding to prepare tender documents.
3.46        Each of the eight provinces has begun to set its priorities
for RHC improvement under the project, within a budget ceiling which
could accommodate a total of four new RHCs or 12 upgraded RHCs, or some
combination of new and upgraded facilities. Following an in-depth
evaluation of the experience with RHCs in FHP1 and with rural health
centers funded by other donors in the early 1980s, a revised flexible
model design has been prepared (see Annex 4). Using this desigii and
based on simple preliminary site surveys and sketch plans, each province
will submit by December 31, 1991 a proposal justifying its choice of
RHCs for construction/upgrading. Criteria will include: local



- 43 -
population to be served, existing facilities and communications, and
availability of staff and recurrent budget. MOH will forward these
proposals to the Bank for review, before finalizing designs and
preparing tender documents.
3.47        The experience of FHPl has highlighted the importance of
staff housing in attracting and retaining MOH health personnel in ;he
more remote districts where resources are to be concentrated in FHP2.
For this reason, funds equal to 252 of the cost of the hospitals will be
set aside for housing. In a typical district with a 78 bed hospital,
housing will include about 10 units for doctors and senior nurses, and
about 15 flatlets for junior nursing personnel. In addition, housing
will be built at new/upgraded RHCs, with the aim of providing at least
three units (for two nurses and one nurse-aide) at each RHC.
3.48        Equipment and furniture will be provided for each district
hospital and RHC. Detailed lists will be drawn up in a three-stage
process, in which several medical equipment consultants will assist MOH
(paras. 3.51 and 3.57). The first step (during the first half of 1991)
will be to review equipment and furniture purchased in FHP1 and the
procurement procedures followed. This will be followed by a detailed
inventory of existing equipment and furniture in the second half of
1991, which will enable MOH to draw up lists for FHP2 districts and
procurement schedules tied to the expected construction timetable, by
June 1992.
Health Manpower Development (base cost US$1.4 million)
3.49         This component will contribute to more effective manpower
planning, training, deployment and management. This has high priority
in MOH, given that 60? of total expenditure is on salaries and wages.
The large volume of training to be carried out under the project - about
2,000 weeks of in-service training involving more than 20,000 persons -
will need careful management, coordination and support. Specif'_
activities to facilitate this include:
(a) Extending and strengthening the inservice training system throughs
preparation and support of district MCH/FP trainers by running
training-of-trainers and Art of Examination courses (or updates)
for all district trainers. (Other support activities for trainers
are included under in the family planning component.) HMD's
coordination and technical and administrative support for
inservice training will be improved by developing a training
information system to maintain records of inservice training,
preparing annual staff development plans and a calendar of
inservice training programs, training those who supervise field
attachments of trainees, running short "guidelines for teaching"
workshops for clinicians who are called on to teach periodically,
and providing books and training equipment for training centers.
The project will support HMD and build its capacity to provide
this improved coordination through a Masters degree in health
education, especially curriculum development, for the training
coordinator, a training advisor to assist the coordinator



- 44 -
especially during her studies, and a sedan car for frequent travel
to and from training sites. The project will also support
curriculum and training materials development, including on-the-
job training by a curriculum specialist who will work with all the
MOH departments with teaching/training responsibilities over the
course of two years.
(b)   Strengthening basic training through developing competency-based
(CB) basic training curricula for all health worker cadres,
training 60 health teachers/tutor/instructors in CB methods, and
developing an induction manual and new teaching modules for the
main basic training programs, providing books fcr district
training facilities, and improving preparation of newly-qualified
doctors for rural practice.
(c)   Building manpower planning and management capacity through
establishing and supporting a task force with representatives of
the Departments of Health Manpower, Health Services Management and
Planning, Finance, Personnel (Staffing), and Nursing. The project
will finance a long term advisor to the task force for 2 years
each, 12 months of local and outside short-term technical
assistance for specific planning and management analyses and other
tasks, a computer, and in-country courses in manpower planning.
Provincial workshops would be held to develop staff performance
indicators, train staff in their use and review their impact.
(d)   Decentralizing personnel administration to provincial and district
levels to strengthen effectiveness and efficiency. This will
require training for personnel staff, equipping provincial
district personnel offices, reproduction of a procedures manual,
and evaluation of the system.
(e) Finalizing and decentralizing the personnel information system
(PIS) to provide regular and up-to-date information on health
staff on which to base management decision-making and planning.
The PIS system which has been set up for the first project pilot
districts needs to be evaluated before proceeding with country-
wide decentralization. Specific activities to be supported by the
project are: planning for decentralization, development and
printing of user manuals, and training in system operation and
informadton use. Computers being provided for PMDs are costed
under the MCH component.
Health Management Strengthening and PMU (base cost US$7.3 million)
3.50        Building upon the successful experience in management
strengthening in FHP1, plus the results of the Bank sector study on
Financing Of Health Services (1989) and a series of reports commissioned
by the Government for the updated Eguity in Health policy paper (1990),
the MOH will aim to develop further management skills and systems under
the second project. The emphasis will be on strengthening selected
areas that are critical to MOH's role as the overall policy-making and
planning body for the health sectox, as well as the major provider of



- 45 -
health, family planning, and nutrition services. As part of the
preparation for the national Five-Year Plan and for FHP2 (which were
carried out simultaneously), MOH has drafted an overall "Management
Strengthening Program" with seven sub-components: materials and
equipment management; health finance reform and financial management5
district health information management; top management and general
management training; health planning; moniitoring and supervisory
systems; and institutional development. While other donors (e.g. SIDA)
will be fundinS parts of this program, FHP2 (utilizing support from ODA,
which is earmarked for management improvement) will play a major role in
implementing the proposed MOH program.
3.51        Materials and Equipment Management  It is now widely
recognized within MOH that the timely availability and efficient
utilization of medical materials and equipment must be vastly improved.
To do so, project resources will help MOH to develop skills and
procedures for planning, ordering, procuring, utilizing, maintaining,
and disposing of equipment and materials. The medical technician
training school at Parirenyatwa Hospital will be expanded, the
curriculum revised, and teaching corps reinforced. Starting in the
second year of the project, 12 technicians will be trained annually
until each province is adequately staffed. These technicians will be
deployed to the provincial medical maintenance teams, whose mobility and
effectiveness will be greatly enhanced through the provision of a
vehicle and a small stock of spare parts for each team. In addition,
the provincial technicians will organize short in-service courses for
the handymen responsible for equipment operations and maintenance at
district hospital level. The module on supplies and materials
management for district health administrators will be revised and
expanded, to improve the DHSAs' ability to supervise the district-based
handymen. To plan and oversee the ertire effort in this area, a medical
equipment maintenance engineer will be recruited externally for three
years, and his local counterpart will be sent for additional training as
required. Terms of reference are given in Annex 6, Part 1.
3.52        Financia. Management  MOH is now committed to decentralizing
budgeting, expenditure decisions, and revenue collection, and to the
development of new budget/expenditure formats that facilitate local
decision-making, make allocation decisions more transparent, and enable
managers better to assess technical efficiency. The Government also
wishes to mobilize additional financial resources for the health sector
by increasing cost recovery, expanding health insurance, and
experimenting with other mechanisms for risk-sharing, local
participation, etc. To do so, MOH will formulate new policies and
eevelop the skills and systems required, utilizing a combination of
local and outside expertise, in-service training, and automation of
financial systems.
3.53        Initially, MOH will concentrate on three health financing
initiativest (a) increasing cost recovery by revising fees and improving
billing and collections; (b) enhancing accounting systems in central
hospitals; and (c) decentralizing budgeting and expenditures to district
level. For each of these initiatives and others that may arise in the
later years of implementation (e.g., drug expenditure monitoring,



- 46 -
expansion of health insurance, etc.), the project will fund short-term
local and foreign technical assistance (a total of 12 person-months),
in-service training, and acquisition of computers and other equipment as
required. To assist in managing the overall process of health finance
reform, a financial management advisor will be engaged (36 person-
months) and will report directly to the Deputy Secretary for Finance and
Administration. Terms of referince for the advisor are given in Annex
6, Part 2. To build local capacity in this area, training in health
economics will be provided through a link arrangement with the London
School of Hygiene and Tropical Medicine. This will include the
establishment in Zimbabwe of a short course in health economics for
senior staff, the training of local trainers, and the award of about
seven scholarskips for overseas study in health economics and/or related
fields.
3.54        MOH has announced that, as part of the structural adjustment
effort, revenue collections from user charges will be increased from 3Z
of recurrent spending in 1990 to at least 10? by 1995. This means a
rise in absolute terms from Z$14 million to Z$60 million over the next
four years. MOH is setting interim targets which will be used to
monitor progress annually. The Ministry is adopting two main strategies
for boosting cost recovery. First, the existing system of user charges
is being clarified through the production and dissemination of new user-
friendly manuals and schedules for use by hospital and RHC personnel, as
well as a broad-based media campaign to inform the public. At the same
time, efforts to enforce the user fee schedule by billing and collecting
charges from patients in a prompt and accurate manner are being
intensified, especially at the four large central hospitals. The second
strategy is to increase the level of MOH fees, which have not been
raised since 1985. Fee increases will be based on the actual rate of
inflation of health care costs, comparable fees being charged by private
hospitals in Zimbabwe, and actual cost-of-service calculations.
3.55        MOH intends to boost revenue collections without increasing
the burden of payment on low-income households. Zimbabwe is perhaps
best-placed among all the African countries to do so, for several
reasons. There is a substantial group of affluent Zimbabweans who
receive medical care from MOH facilities and are covered by health
insurance schemes that will reimburse the Ministry, as long as billing
and collection are carried out effectively; this group will account for
most of the increase in user fees paid. At the same time, Zimbabwe
already has a system for exempting low-income households earning lese
than Z$150 per month, and this system is currently being enhanced by
having the Department of Social Welfare play an expanded role in
identifying and certifying low-income families as exempted from paying
fees.
3.56        District Health Information Management  While information
systems at district level have been greatly improved in recent years
through a number of separate initiatives (e.g., National Health
Information System, personnel MIS, essential drugs program, etc.),
information management is still seriously deficient in two respects -
the lack of integration of data for decision-making, and the slow speed
of information retrieval and processing using manual systems. To



- 47 -
overcome these weaknesses, FHP2 resources will be used to pilot and then
extend nationally an integrated and computerized district health
information system. MUH staff and short-term consultants (total of 12
person-months) will design the new system, train district personnel, and
then progressively test and upgrade the system. Funds will be provided
for 24 computers (one per target district and one per province) to run
the system, and for the printing of manuals and other training
materials. Through link arrangements with UK health institutions,
practical experience in MIS development (integration of various data
sub-systems, selection of progress indicators, design of user manuals,
etc) will be applied to Zimbabwe's own health information needs.
3.57        Top Management and General Management Training  Since the
organizational and management changes proposed by MOH will only be fully
effective if they are well understood and actively promoted by senior
officials of the Ministry, the project will work intensively with the
MOH top management team in this area. An organizational development
consultant (total of 4 person-months) will meet periodically with the
team to conduct in-service training and identify other training needs,
including short attachments to other relevant health institutions
outside Zimbabwe. Through this process, the top management team will
elaborate a strategy and plan for enhancing the MOH work culture and
improving Ministry-wide procedures and practices, and will subsequently
develop performance indicators and monitor the results. To complement
the SIDA-funded mid-level management training, FHP2 will support an
assessment of management skills needs and an evaluation of the District
Heelth Services Administrators course set up under FHPi. The DHSA
course and other management training activities will then be modified
accordingly.
3.58        To further enhance the planning, implementation, and
-valuation of public health activities in Zimbabwe and in Southern
PI-ica generally, project funds will be utilized to establish a masters
degree program in public health at the University of Zimbabwe,
Department of Community Medicine. ODA support will cover a link
arrangement with a suitable UK institutions, technical assistance from a
senior lecturer/associate professor, training equipment and materials,
and a small fund for operations research on vital public health topics.
3.59        Project Management Unit  To coordinate and monitor the
project, including the maintenance of consolidated project accounts,
disbursement records, annual work plans and progress reports, the
Project Management Unit (PMU) established for the first Family Health
Project will continue under FHP2. Weaknesses in coordination of the
first project (e.g., inadequate planning and accounting, difficulties in
medical equipment procurement and hospital commissioning, delays in
claiming donor reimbursements) will be overcome by expanding the PMU
staff, providing on-the-job training and short-term technical assistance
early in the project period, and establishing effective management
systems at the outset of the new project (para. 5.04).
3.60        The existing PMU staff (project manager, deputy manager,
commissioning officer, accountant, and secretary, plus two local
physical planning consultants) will be maintained under FHP2. In



- 48 -
addition, project funds will be utilized to hire on contract several
local staff who cannot be recruited through regular public service
procedures: a financial officer, procurement officer, and an executive
secretary/clerk. MOH has agreed to hire a long-term (36 person-months)
physical planner/architect externally to assist with the hospital and
RHC construction program (see Annex 4, Part 3 for terms of reference).
Short-term technical assistance in project management (4 person-months)
and equipment procurement (6 person-months) will also be provided in the
first and second years of the project. Terms of reference for these
consultants are given in Annex 6, Parts 3 and 4. To facilitate the
PMU's work, project funds will be utilized to purchase a 5-ton track for
hospital commissioning, a four-wheel drive vehicle and a sedan car for
field supervision, microcomputers and associated software, and other
office equipment. Two drivers will be assigned to the PMU. A project
launch workshop for 80-100 participants will be held just prior to
project effectiveness, to ensure that implementing officers are well
briefed and have developed work plans and reporting systems for the
first year of the project.

IV. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. Costs
4.1         The total cost of the project is Z$363.0 million or US$116.9
million equivalent. Base costs are estimated at US$99.0 million equivalent
(852) and contingencies at US$17.9 million equivalent (152). Foreign exchange
costs are estimated at US$56.8 million or 49? of total project costs. Taxes
and duties are negligible, being less than 1S of total project costs. All
costs are based on November 1990 prices. Project costs by component are
summarized in Table 4.1.
4.2         Project costs by expenditure category are shown in Annex 1, Table
1. Capital costs for civil works account for 57? of base costs; furniture,
equipment, vehicles and supplies and materials for 22Z; training for 7?;
technical assistance for 5?; research, surveys, monitoring and evaluation for
12; and incremental recurrent costs (salaries and allowances, utilities and
supplies, and operation and maintenance of buildings, equipment and vehicles)
for 7}.
4.3         Civil works costs have been estimated on the basis of the value of
current contracts for comparable buildings. For most district hospital
buildings, construction costs are based on the rate of US$560 per sq. m.,
rising to US$760 per sq. m. for special facilities such as X-Ray and operating
theater. Staff housing is estimated at 25? of the cost of hospital buildings;
external works/location factor has been costed at 20? of building
expenditures; hospital equipment and furniture at 14? and 6? respectively of
civil works costs, based on the experience of FHPI; and architectural fees at
10? of building costs. The a/erage cost of an RHC upgrading is US$90,000,
with new health centers estimateed to cost about US$210,000. The cost of
furniture, supplies, and other (non-hospital) equipment is based on contracts
for similar items recently awarded by MOH, quotations from suppliers, and
international catalogs (including the UNICEF UNIPAC catalog). Equipment lists
will be finalized after a thorough review of the hospital and clinic equipment
purchased under the FHP1. The project includes 24 ambulances and 70 outreach
and supervisory vehicles, at a unit cost of US$27,600 (CIF), 10 mini-buses for
transporting student nurses and nutrition trainees at a unit cost of
US$32,400, and 90 motorcycles for MOH and 190 for the CBD system at an average
unit cost of US$1,020; 8.5 years of overseas degree study at an average annual
cost of US$25,000 and 25 short courses overseas at an average cost of US$8,000
per course (including airfare) and 12 study tours. Technical assistance costs
include airfares, fees and allowances. There are 16 years of long-term
technical assistance which is expected to be recruited abroad, 15 years likely
to be recruited locally or in the region, and 11 staffyears for clerk-of-works
with foreman qualifications; and 72 months of short-term technical assistance
likely to be recruited in the region (including Zimbabwe itself) and 44 months
for short-term consultants to be recruited abroad. Incremental salaries are
based on current Government scales, and local training costs and operational
and maintenance expenses are based on current actual MOH costs.
