4.5         The foreign exchange component of US$56.8 was calculated on the
following basis: (a) civil works - 402; (b) furniture - 501; (c) equipment -
100; (d) vehicles - 901; (e) supplies and materials - 50X; (f) technical
assistance - 80X; (g) training and workshops - 302; (h) overseas training and
study tours - 100Z; (i) research - 501. For incremental recurrent costs, the
foreign exchange components were estimated at 301 for local wage costs; 601
for vehicle operating and maintenance costs; and 20% for building maintenance.
4.6         Recurrent Costs  Serious efforts have been made to minimize the
incremental recurrent costs associated with the project, and to ensure that
they will be affordable within the tightly constrained budget projected for
the MOH during (and beyond) the project life. The designs and size of
hospitals and clinics have been very carefully reviewed and modified so as to
minimize investment and recurrent costs, without compromising the quality of
health care; the number of new staff posts within MOH and ZNFPC has been
limited to a handful of high-level managers and technicians and critical
outreach workers in three areas which are seriously understaffed at present
(family planning, nutrition, and health education); and transport and
equipment have been restricted to those items required to support programs in
the field.
4.7         Furthermore, the project includes several activities designed to
offset higher incremental spending, by containing costs through improved
efficiency, and by increasing cost recovery and resource flows into the
sector. Part of the expected increase in the MOH budget is predicated on the
health sector recovering a larger share of costs through fee increases and
strengthened billing and collections. Cost recovery is expected to rise from
less than 31 of MOH expenditure in 1990 to 10 in 1995: in that year, Z$55-60
million is projected to be collected by the Ministry.
4.8         Analysis of the recurrent costs associated with FHP2 suggests that
these can be accommodated within the expected increase in the MOH recurrent
budget over the next five years. Incremental costs connected with the
upgrading of 16 district hospitals, which will constitute the bulk of
incremental expenditure resulting from FHP2, will amount to Z$14-15 million by
1995/96. Including other recurrent costs associated with the project (e.g.,
at upgraded rural health centers, vehicle operating costs, salaries for a
small number of other additional MOH and ZNFPC staff at headquarters and
provincial levels), total recurrent costs due to FHP2 will amount to about
Z$20 million annually by the end of the project period. The main scenario
currently being used by MFEPD and World Bank staff assisting in the



- 52 -
preparation of the Structural Adjustment Program shows a real increase in MOH
spending of Z$134 million by 1995/96. Under this scenario, the expected Z$20-
25 million in extra MO  spending as a result of PHP2 would be equal to just
15-192 of the incremental MOH budget.  Under a more pessimistic scenario of
3.02 real growth in the MOH budget, additional spending due to FHP2 would
still absorb less than a third of MOH incremental resources.
4.9         Whether the projected MO  recurrent budget will be adequate to
meet the overall needs of the health sector, including YHP2, will depend on a
number of other factors outside of PHP2 itself. These include:
(a)   The degree of 'underfunding' of existing servicess  If all existing MOH
vacancies were filled, for example, the salary bill would be Z$10.9
million higher.  The current MOH budget for drugs (Z$46.4 million) also
appears to be inadequate; an additional Z$10 million is needed to cover
this shortfall. Spending trends for other expenditure categories for
the first part of 1990-91 also point to some degree of underfunding; an
additional Z$5-l0 million is required to meet these costs. It is widely
agreed that the current MOH transport fleet is inadequate; if the
recommended program for rebuilding the fleet (400 extra vehicles) were
implemented, an additional Z$7 million in arnual operating costs would
be needed.
(b)   Secular growth in drug consumption as a result of improved health care,
increased personal incomes, and the spread of HIV-related illnesses: It
is extremely difficult to estimate this secular trend accurately,
especially since current efforts by the ZEDAP to improve drug storage,
prescribing practices and consumption and to lower unit costs through
better procurement and foreign exchange allocation should result in
important savings for drugs. Under a possible scenario in which secular
changes h-i drug consumption amount to 52 a year, an additional Z$19
million would be required by 1995 to meet total MOH drug requirements.
(c)   Other planned expansion of MOH servicesz The Government has at times in
recent years expressed its interest in building a large new provincial
hospital in Chinhoyi, and in constructing Multidisciplinary Training
Centers in several provincial capitals. Several other hospital
construction projects have also been proposed by MOH and will probably
be included in the PSIP. However, with the exception of a new maternity
wing at Mpilo Hospital (which has secured Japanese financing), these
other capital projects do not have foreign funding at present. Since in
the past the Government's contribution to the MOH capital budget
(outside of FHPl) has been very small, it is unlikely that these other
hospital programs will go forward during the coming five years, unless
donor assistance is forthcoming.
4.10        If all the incremental recurrent costs mentioned above are
combined (ZS20 million for FHP2, Z$10 million for drug underfunding and Z$19
million for secular increases in drug consumption, Z$l1 million for filling
vacant posts, Z$10 million for "other expenditures', and Z$7 million for
vehicle operation), the expected increase in recurrent expenditure amounts to
Z$77 million.  While this would be well within the MOH incremental budget if
the budget grows at Government's 5.32 average redl growth rate, it would
absorb nearly all of the budget if it grew at its recent historical average of
32 a year.  Under these circumstances, the MO1  is rightly expressing caution
about embarking on any other major capital projects during the coming five



- 53 -
year period. It is doubtful that a large hospital construction program such
as the one being proposed for Chinhoyi, or a major expansion of health
training at new multi-disciplinary schools, could be sustained during the
five-year plan period.
B. FinancinR
4.11        The total project cost of US$116.9 million equivalent, minus taxes
and duties, will be financed by the Government, the World Bank, and a number
of multilateral and bilateral sources (the financing plan is shown in Table 4-
2 below). The IBRD loan of US$25.0 million will cover 21.42 of total project
cost and the equivalent of 44Z of the foreign exchange cost, and will be
utilized primarily for district hospital upgrading and purchase of medical
equipment. The Government contribution of Z$171.5 million (US$55.2 million)
or 47.2Z of the total, will finance part of the cost of hospital and
ruralhealth center upgrading, local expenditures on furniture, supplies and
materials, services, training, research and studies, some imported equipment
and costs of training abroad and the incremental costs of salaries and
allowances and of operations and maintenance of vehicles, equipment, and
buildings.
4.12        Co-financing from other external sources will include;
(a) US$3.7 million equivalent from the European Community, for upgrading rural
health delivery in Kariba district; (b) US$8.3 million equivalent from DANIDA
for family planning training, IEC activities, FP management strengthening, and
for health manpower development; (c) US$14.2 million equivalent from the
Norwegian Government, for midwifery, MCH equipment and IEC, telecommunications
equipment, district ambulances and outreach vehicles, iodine deficiency
monitoring and testing and nutrition staff development, rural health center
construction, equipment for the district hospitals and rural health centers,
family planning clinical service and Youth Advisory Service expansion,
monitoring and evaluation and the PMU; (d) US$5.8 million from the Netherlands
for nutrition and expanding FP outreach services; and (e) US$4.7 million from
the British Overseas Development Agency for health management strengthening.

4.14        The major civil works contracts for construction/upgrading of 14-
15 district hospitals, to be financed by the World Bank and the Government,
will be awarded on the basis of international competitive bidding (aggregate
cost US$60.2 million, Bank financing of US$21.2 million). Upgrading of one or
two district hospitals, to be financed by the EC, would be awarded on the
basis of international competition according to EC procedures. Civil works
contracts for construction/upgrading of RHCs, with an average value of US$1.6
million per province, will form part of the hospital contracts awarded on the
basis of ICB. While the major contractors may in some cases sub-contract to



- 55 -
smaller firms for the RHCa, overall responsibility will remain with the main
contractor. This will limit the difficulties experienced under the first
project in administering a large number of small contracts and in evaluating
the capacity of medium-sized local contractors to carry out the work. Other
project civil works (e.g., minor improvements to nurse training and equipment
technicians schools, construction of youth counselling centers, installation
of water supply in rural health facilities, aggregate cost US$5 million, Bank
financing US$1 million) entail small geographically dispersed works that would
not attract international firms. These works, to be financed mainly by the
Netherlands and Norway through trust fund arrangements with the Bank, will be
carried out using local competitive bidding procedures which are satisfactory
to the Bank.
4.15        Equipment, supplies, and vehicles financed by the World Bank,
Norway, and Netherlands will be grouped as far as possible into contracts
valued at US$100,000 or more and awarded on the basis of international
competitive bidding (ICB) procedures according to IBRD guidelines. Total ICB
procurement of goods will aggregate to about US$17.3 million, with other
donors providing the bulk of the financing and Bank providing about US$1.8
million. For goods tenders, local manufacturers will be eligible for a margin
of preference of 152 as compared to the c.i.f. price of competing imports, or
the prevailing customs duty, whichever is lower. Equipment and supplies items
and vehicles which cannot be grouped into bid packages of US$100,000 or more
and are to be financed by the World Bank, Netherlands, and Norway will be
procured through local competitive bidding (LCB) procedures. Contracts for
furniture and for IEC mass media production will also be awarded through LCB;
mainly because of the large amount of furniture and media production in the
project, contracts awarded by LCB are expected to have an aggregate value of
about US$4.5 million, with Bank financing of about US$0.5 million. Local
procurement procedures have been reviewed by the World Bank and found to be
acceptable. Small off-the-shelf items (e.g., print materials, office
supplies, costing less than US$25,000 per contract that cannot be bulked into
LCB or ICB packages or which are required urgently may be procured though
"prudent shopping" by comparing prices obtained from three independent
suppliers. Items procured in this manner will amount to about US$2.4 million,
with about USO.5 million of this financed by the Bank. Equipment and supplies
to be financed by other donors who are providing funds on a parallel basis
(DANIDA, EC, and ODA) will be procured in accordance with their own
procedures.
4.16        Consultants' services to be financed by NORAD and Netherlands will
be procured in accordance with "Guidelines for the Use of Consultants by World
Bank Borrowers and the World Bank as an Executing Agency". Consultants to be
made available directly by ODA and DANIDA would be recriuited according to the
respective donor procedures.
4.17        Contract Review  Bidding packages for ICB civil works (i.e.,
district hospital construction) and for ICB vehicles, equipment, and materials
(valued at more than US$100,000 equivalent) will be subject to World Bank
prior review of procurement documentation, including tender documents,
evaluation reports, and draft contracts. Smaller packages will be subject to
random post-award review by the World Bank. The PMU will therefore be
required to keep full procurement documentation on file for easy access. For
technical assistance to be financed by Norway and Netherlands, invitations for
proposals, firms' submissions, evaluation reports, and draft contracts will be
reviewed by the World Bank prior to award. Similarly, for individual



- 56 -
consultancies, the Bank will review terms of reference, curriculum vitae, and
draft letters of appointment before such appointments are made.

will be made against certified statements of expenditure (S0E). Suppor .ing
documentation will be retained by project executing agencies and will be
available for review as requested by the World Bank supervision missions and

I. Accounts and Audit
4.20        Special Account.  In order to ensure that funds for the project
will be available as needed, a Special Account operated by the Project
Management Unit will be established by Government through the Reserve Bank of
Zimbabwe with a commercial bank. This account will receive deposits from the
project loan for financing eligible project expenditures (for items described
in the above section D). An initial amount of US$250,000 will be deposited by
the World Bank after receipt and approval of a withdrawal application. This
amount is estimated to cover about the maximum four-month average of



- 57 -
expenditure. to be financed from the account. Replenishment applications for
the Special Account will be submitted monthly or as required and will be
accompanied by the full documentation except for items eligible for
disbursement on the basis of certified statements of expenditure (SOE).
Similar special accounts may also be established to handle donor funds from
the Netherlands and Norway administered by the World Bank under trust fund
arrangements.
4.21        Audit  The Government's performance in auditing project accounts
under FHPl has generally been acceptable. Delays in carrying out audits that
were experienced in the early years of the project have now been overcome, in
large part by engaging additional PHU staff to prepare project accounts.  At
present, FHPl audits are up-to-date. The quality of the audits has also been
satisfactory. For FHP2, a project financial officer will be added to the PHU,
to established and monitor separate detailed accounts for all project
expenditures incurred by MOH and other implementing Government agencies.
Assurances were given by Government that all project accounts will be audited
by the Auditor General and that all audited accounts, together with the
auditors' report, will be subm'tted to the World Bank not later than nine
months after the end of each Gcvernment fiscal year. In auditing FHP2
accounts, the auditors will provide a separate opinion on SOEs and on the
procedures and internal controls involved in their preparation. Project
special accounts will also be audited. ZNFPC will continue to submit its
accounts, ircluding those items expressly identified as being financed from
FHP2, to independent private auditors for annual audit, and will forward their
report to the World Bank through MOH, according to the timetable specified
above.

V. PROJECT IILLIMNTATION
A. Organization and Management
5.1         General  The lead agency for project implementation will be
MOH. Within MOH, direct responsibility for execution of specific
components and subcomponents will be assumed by the relevant MOH
headquarters departments and units within ZNFPC. The Provincial Health
Teams and the District Health Teams for the 16 target districts will
play a key role in ensuring the coordination and integration of project
activities on the ground, consistent with annual work plans and budgets
prepared by both the provinces/districts and national program offices.
5.2         In addition to MOH and ZNFPC, a number of other Government
and NGO agencies will assist in project implementation, including the
Ministry of Public Construction and National Housing (rural health
facilities construction and upgrading); the Ministry of Water and Energy
(water supplies to hospitals and RHCs); the Ministry of Cooperative and
Community Development (village community workers)s the Ministry of
Education (school health); the Ministry of Agriculture (Community Food
and Nutrition Program); and institutions belonging to the Zimbabwe
Association of Church Hospitals (ZACH). Their involvement in the
project will be coordinated by the Project Management Unit and the
Project Advisory Committee (para. 5.5 and 5.8).
5.3         The project has been designed by the Government to be
implemented through the regular organizational structure of KOH and
other agencies, with a minimum of project-specific organizational
development. The same MOH departments that have already taken the lead
in preparing the project will be expected to carry out the proposed
project activities. Primary and secondary responsibility for execution
of project activities is shown in Table 5-1.
5.4         While FHP2 is a major investment project, covering a ;urge
part of Zimbabwe's planned capital expenditure for health, population,
and nutrition over the next five years, proposed organizational
arrangements have been carefully assessed and found to be
institutionally feasible. The Government's demonstrated capacity to
plan and design in detail the proposed project shows that MOR and other
implementing agencies will bring a high level of commitment and
competence to project implementation. Day-to-day implementation
responsibility has been delegated to the concerned MOH departments and
provincial/district offices that were centrally involved in project
preparation. Many of these units have already proven their ability to
execute similar project activities under FHPI. Where weaknesses in
implementation capacity have been identified, these are being addressed
through a combination of training and short-term technical assistance.
to ensure that performance in specific components does not fall behind,
and that inter-departmental coordination is effective, KOH will be
establishing a Project Advisory Committee and holding regular meetings
with provincial and district officials (para. 5.7).

5.5            Project Management  Overall responsibility for project
coordination will rest with the Project Management Unit (PMU), composed of
a project manager, deputy manager, financial officer, accountant, physical
planner, commissioning officer, procurement officer, secretaries and
drivers. The PMU will also be assisted by a consultant advisor for
physical planning/architecture (para. 3.59). Most of these positions
already exist under FHP1 and are currently filled; the incumbents are
expected to continue under FHP2, and only three new posts (financial
officer, procurement officer, physical planning advisor) will need to be
filled. The appointment of persons acceptable to the World Bank for the
posts of financial officer and procurement officer and recruitment of the
architect/physical planning advisor will be a condition of effectiveness.



- 60 -
5.6            The project manager will report directly to the Permanent
Secretary for Health. The main tasks of the PMU will be to: (a) organize
and m"pervise the drafting of annual work plans and progress reports for
various components; (b) consolidate these work plans and reports for review
by the MOH and donors; (c) organize quarterly meetings of the Project
Advisory Committee (para. 5.05); (d) monitor overall implementation and
help eliminate bottlenecks as they arise; (e) review spending decisions for
the maternal and child health, rural health delivery, and manpower
components; and (f) maintain overall project accounts and prepare
reimbursement applications.
5.7            The Ministry of Public Construction and National Housing
will be directly responsible for implementation of project buildings, on
behalf of the user ministry, MOH. The Ministry of Construction will engage
and oversee the work of private architectural firms in the design of
facilities, and will subsequently supervise the work of building
contractors. To supplement local supervisory capabilities, MPCNH will need
to recruit externally a limited number of clerks-of-works, as under the
first project. A total of 11 person-years will be required, somewhat less
than the amount provided in the first project. Likewise, the Ministry of
Water and Energy will carry out the provision of water supplies to health
facilities. The ZNFPC will be in charge of the implementation of the
family planning component of FHP2, with authority to operate and special
account and claim donor reimbursements (para. 4.20). All of the above-
mentioned agencies will submit period progress reports to the PMU.
5.8            The Project Advisory Committee which has functioned
satisfactorily under FHP1 will continue to operate under the new project,
meeting on a quarterly basis to review implementation performance and
discuss future work plans. As in FHPI, the committee will be composed of
managers of all the components/sub-components and representatives of the
provinces. The PAC's terms of reference are given in Arnex 6, Part 5.
B. Status of Project
5.9            Since the project has been prepared in considerable detail
by a wide range of headquarters and field units of MOB and ZNFPC, most
component activities have already been scheduled for the five-year project
period and are virtually ready for implementation. Work plans and budgets
for the first year of the project will be drawn up during the pre-
implementation period and wil be reviewed and finalized at the time of
project launch.
5.10           For project-supported training, timetables ave been
prepared, curricula have generally been revised (e.g., in-service family
planning, midwifery, middle level management, health administrators), and
adequate facilities and tutors are available. The curricula for health
economics trairzng and for medical equipment technicians training are to be
prepared, tested, and revised iL the first year of project implementation.
5.11           Take   of r'ference for long-term technical assistance have
already been drawn up and will form the basis for recruitment (Annezes 2-



- 61 -
6). Scopes of work for the key operational research studies have also been
drafted. Several long-term consultants, including the architect/physical
planning advisor and the financial management advisor, are to be recruited
in the first half of 1991 and should be in post prior to project
effectiveness; during the pre-impiementation period, they will be financed
from other sources including FHP1 and corsultant trust funds.
5.12           For project civil works, the standard designs for district
hospitals in FHP1 have been reviewed and modified designs have been
developed and discussed thoroughly with users (Annex 4, Part 4). The new
designs have been applied to the FHP2 districts in a highly flexible
manner. As a result of visits to all districts and proposed sites by MOM
and architectural consultants, preliminary sketch designs and estimates for
these facilities, as well as master plans for future development, are
already prepared.  Ministry of Public Construction will, through private
architects, develop designs and tender documents for the new stock-type
design by June 30, 1991. This will permit donor review during the pre-
implementation period. Although sites have been identified in all project
districts, they will only be finalized once more detailed surveys have been
carried out to confirm availability of water, suitability of sewerage
infiltration, ground conditions for foundations, etc. Once this is done,
local private architects will also be engaged to prepare final design
drawings, as in FHP1. MOH will recruit an architectlphysicial planning
advisor during the pre-implementation period, in order to assist the
Ministry in the design and construction phases of the project.
5.13           In order to spread the workload, implementation will be
phased starting with four hospitals in the first year, six in the second,
and six in the third. Packaging for any given year will be based on
expressed need and geographical factors. The districts hospitals have
already been tentatively classified according to need:
Group I: no existing hospital or current facility totally unacceptable:
Siakobvu (Kariba), Nkayi (Nkayi), Chimanda (Rushinga), Kotwa (Mudzi),
Mutawatawa (UMP), Hauna (Mutasa,, and Guruve (Guruve);
Group II: present facilities inadequate in relation to population served:
Chibi (Chibi), Matibi (Mwanezi), Silobela (Kwekwe), and Sadza (Chivhu);
Group III: hospitals fully functional but require some additional units:
Kadoma (Kadoma), Murambinda (Buhera), Plumtree (Bulalimamangwe), and
Inyathi (Bubi);
Group IVs replacement will only become necessary when existing hospital
site is flooded: Antelope (Kezi-Matobo).
Based on the above groupings and their physical location, appropriate
packaging of hospital construction contracts has already been suggested.
Tender documents for the first four hospital contracts will be finalized by
October 31, 1991. Tendering will follow, to ensure that construction
commences in early 1992 (see the timetable given in Annex 4, Part 2).



- 62 -
5.14           Based on the experience of PHPI, stringent cost control
procedures will be followed for health facilities upgrading/construction in
the proposed project:
-  detailed surveys of sites and existing buildings must be properly
carried out, including a full soil investigation;
-  all architectural consultants to the project must have professional
liability insurance;
-  tender documents must be sufficiently detailed to keep the need for
provibional sum to a minimum of not more than 10-152 of the total
estmatud cost; and
-  all variation orders with cost implications of more than an agreed sum,
e.g., Z$l0,OO0, should be approved by MOH before being issued.
5.15     Based on a thorough review of RHC upgrading in FHPI (based on
repeated field visits and a special architectural assessment in late 1990),
the standard designs for RHCs have been modified. New plans are based on a
standard size room unit (3.6 X 4.8 meters) with flexible and multiple
functions. A list of locations for 12 new and 54 upgraded RHCs has been
drawn up by MOH. This may require some revision before finalization, but
all target districts are aware that their final selection with be
constrained by a fixed provincial budget wLlocation for RHC improvements.
All project districts, through their provincial medical offices, will
submit by June 30, 1991 a list of proposed locations for REC
constructionjupgrading, following which the Ministry of Construction and
the private architects engaged under the project will carry out detailed
site surveys and prepare drawings, estimates, and tender documents. Based
on the uneven performance of small independent contractors in FHP1, REC
upgrading will be included in the district hospital contracts under FHP2,
thus making the major contractors formally responsible.
5.16           Equipment for district hospitals and RHCs will follow the
standard lists developed for FHP1, and revised in the light of the
evaluation of equipment purchase and utilization in the first project.
This evaluation has now been completed. Equipment lists for maternity
services have been reviewed and finalized. Equipment for voluntary
surgical contraception will follow the standard package developed under
FHP1. Equipment needs for management strengthening (computers, office
equipment) have already been quantified, and a procurement timetable has
been developed.
5.17           Vehicle lists and procurement schedules have already been
drawn up, and bulk purchases will be effected in line with these schedules.
Vehicle procurement, carried out by MOH with technical advice from CMED,
has proceeded smoothly in FHP1 and is not expected to creatt any
difficulties in FHP2. However, current arrangements for maintenance and
repair of MOH vehicles, including those purchased under FHP1, are not
satisfactory. The CMED, which is responsible for vehicle maintenance, has
experienced frequent delays in servicing MO1 vehicles, mainly because of
shortages of skilled manpower, workshop facllities, and spare parts.
Donor-financed spare parts for MOH projects have been mixed with other CHED



- 63 -
spares and have frequently been out of stock when required for MOH project
vehicles. Recognizing many of these problems, CMED has recently indicated
to MOH that it intends to improve its level of service by carrying out a
series of reforms, including (a) increasing staff and staff remunertions
(b) improving spare parts inventory management; and (c) making more
frequent use of private garages, especially for MOH ambulances. Assurances
were given by Government that CMED will by March 31, 1992 provide to MOH
and the World Bank a plan containing its proposed new arrangements for
maintenance and repair of MOH vehicles, covering the management and
reporting of spare parts inventories, service quality and turnaround times,
and sub-contracting with private garages.  Following consultation with MOH
and the Bank, CMED will implement the agreed plan by July 1, 1992.
5.18           Project implementation is expected to take five years,
starting in September 1991. The project will be completed by December 31,
1996 and will close on December 31, 1997. Most project activities will get
under way in the first year of the project, with the exception of (a)
district hospital and RHC construction, which will be phased over the
project life, and (b) full-scale implementation of the community-based
contraceptive depot-holder scheme, which will begin in the third year.
C. Monitoring. Reporting, and Evaluation
5.19           The project manager and his unit will have the overall
responsibility for project monitoring, with each implementing
department/agency expected to monitor directly and report to the PMU on
progress in specific project components and subcomponents. The PMU and the
implementing departments/agencies will prepare and finalize at the time of
the launch workshop draft formats for monitoring the project. These will
be forwarded to the World Bank for review. Implementation monitoring will
assess financial and physical progress of the various components, with the
most meaningful and practical indicators selected. The main national and
district targets set at the beginning of the project (para. 3.02) will be
closely monitored. Monitoring data will be sent to the PMU on a quarterly
basis for inclusion in the consolidated quarterly and annual progress
reports. Assurances were given by the Govermnent that semi-annual reiwrts
will be consolidated by the PMU within six weeks of the end of each
semester, reviewed by the ProJect Advisory Comm.ttee and forwarded to IDA
within eight weeks of the end of each semester.
5.20           In addition to the monitoring data collected by implementing
agencies as part of routine management, the project will also draw upon the
results of several other surveys and studies. These will include a follow-
up to the Demographic and Health Survey of 1988, to takz place in 1992 and
the annual reviews of the Expanded Program of Immunization and other
primary health care programs conducted by UNICEF. Furthermore, funds will
be provided under the project for a series of ad hoc diagnostic and
evaluation studies of specific project components, e.g., effectiveness of
midwifery training and maternal health activities, contraceptive user
satisfaction, public response to mass media campaigns, effectiveness of
child nutrition interventions. These operational research funds will for
the most part be attached to specific components, and the PHU will also
keep a small contingency fund for this purpose.



- 64 -
5.21           Starting with the pre-implementation period and the project
launch, implementing departmentslagencies will prepare annual work plans
and budgets, and the PMU will combine these in a single volume for review
by the Project Coordination Coumittee and the donors. As project
effectiveness is expected to take place in September 1991, at the mid-point
of the Zimbabwean financial year, it may be advisable to prepare a *mini
work plans for the first six months of the project and then to have
subsequent annual plans coincide with the July-June fiscal year, starting
in July 1992. Assurances were given at negotiations that MOB will submit
to the World Bank by May 15 of each year the annual work ilan for the
following fiscal year.
5.22           To ensure that a complete stock-taking of project
implementation performance and expected development Impact is carried out
during the project lifetime, the Government, Bank, and other co-financing
partners vill conduct a mid-term evaluation of the project some time in
1994. The results of such an in-depth review will help to make mid-course
improvements in implementation, as was the case under FHP1.
5.23           The Government will prepare a project completion report
within six months of the completion date, based upon a format to be agreed
with the World Bank.



- 65 -
VI. PROJECT BEWFITS, JUSTIFICATION. AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Justification
6.1         Proceeding with the project as proposed by the Government is
clearly justified, to the extent that FHPI will tackle many of the key
constraints to the health, nutrition, and population sectors in
Zimbabwe. These constraints include:
-     inadequate promotion and delivery of non-pill family planning
methods and, in some parts of the country and for some groups
(e.g., youth), lack of family planning services of any kind;
-     excessive maternal morbidity and mortality;
-     a significant amount of easily preventable child malnutrition,
especially those cases associated with child care and feeding
practices and micronutrient deficiencies;
-     lack of access to good quality primary and first-line referral
health care;
-     weak and overly-centralized systems for management of financial,
human, and material resources for the health sector; and
-     low levels of cost recovery and, more broadly, of internally-
generated resources for health.
The project will help largely to overcome these constraints and will
assist the Government in achieving its basic sectoral objectives as
enunciated in the updated "Eauity in Health' paper, i.e., to improve the
quality of, and access to, basic health and family planning services for
the majority of the country's population. To do so, the Government
envisions an important role for NGOs and the private sector, with the
MOH, local councils, and municipal government ensuring that no one is
denied these basic services.
6.2         To better appreciate the anticipated benefits of FHP2, it
may be useful to consider the likely health, nutrition, and population
status of Zimbabwe in the absence of such a project. Without FHP2, it
is probable that the use of family plannin8 services would expand rather
sluggishly, and certainly not to the target level of 55Z of married
women of reproductive age. There is evidence, in fact, that family
planning usage levels have become stationary in recent years, and would
probably remain fairly stagnant without the sort of method mix campaign
(training, IEC, outreach, etc.) to be carried out under the project.
Similarly, child malnutrition would decline only gradually in the
absence of the project, especially since stunting and micronutrient
deficiencies are linked to environmental and cultural factors that will
not change dramatically with economlc growth. A concerted effort to
modify child care and feeding practices and to fortify foods with



- 66 -
micronutrients, of the kind proposed under FHP2. Without the project,
the maternal mortality might well remin virtually stationary, with only
a minor decline linked to increased use of famLly plannlng and lower
fertillty. The impact on maternal mortalLty of midwifery traLnlng for
nurses, better equLppLng of RHCs, and provLiLon of emergency transport
and communications, under the project should be conslderable.
6.3         In the absence of FHP2, the general health status of the
populatlon in the 16 taxrget dlstricts would continue to improve, in
response to general lncxeases in household income and assocLated
improvements ln liet, %ousing, and water and sanitation. However, the
complementary and reLnforcing effects of having trained, well-equipped,
and properly housed and motivated staff to dellver family plannlng,
maternal and chlld health, and basic curative servLces would be lost
without the provlslon of essential rural health infrastructure in the 16
districts. These include several districts that lack even a sLngle
referral hospltal and would almost certainly be forced to continue
sendlng patients elsewhere if such facillties were not built under the
project. Finally, without FHP2, crltlcal health cadres (especially
maternity-trained nuzses, equipment technicians, family planning
workers) would remain seriously understaffed wlth negative effects on
the availability and quality of servlces. Even though some important
management skills would be imparted to health managers through training
programs supported by SIDA, several key management domains (financLal
and equipment/materials management in particular) would continue to be
neglected in the absence of FHP2, with attendant adverse impacts on
availability and efficiency of health servlces.
6.4         Given the very wide scope of the project, a substantial
number of Zlmbabweans will benefit directly from it. About 55Z of women
of reproductive age, or 870,000 persons, will receive better quality
family planning services and about 190,000 of these will be utilizing
non-pill methods of contraception by 1996, largely because of project-
supported actlvities. A large proportion of the 250,000 women
delivering babies in health institutions in 1996 will benefit from safer
care provided by maternity nurses trained and equipped under the
project. Roughly 200,000 children aged 0-5 will be reached by nutrition
activities under PHP2. As a result of improvements in rural health
infrastructure in the 16 target districts, virtually the entire
population of these selected areas, or about 2.2 million persons - many
of these belonging to low-lncome rural households - will have access to
good primary and preventive health services and to basic curative care
in RHCs and district hospitals.
6.5         Health, nutrition, and family planning workers trained under
the project will also benefit directly by acquiring skills and related
employment and income-earnlng opportunities. These lnclude the 8,000
health workers to be trained in family planning, the 2,500 nurses
scheduled to receive maternity training, 180 persons to be trained as
nutrLtion coordinators, Institutional Domestic Supervisors and
nutritionists, 36 equipment technicians to be taught at Parirenyatwa,
and many others who will attend short courses under FHP2.



- 67 -
6.6         A key benefit accruing to the Government of Zimbabwe vill be
the enormous expansion of local capacity to plan and manage health,
nutrition, and family planning services over the coming decades. Skills
and systems will be greatly enhanced in the areas oft health sector
planning; budgeting, accounting and revenue collection; personnel
management and manpower planning; equipment procurement and maintenance;
and hospital administration. Furthermore, as a result of changes in the
level and structure of user charges and in billing and collection
practices, MOH will increase cost recovery from about 32 of annual
recurrent expenditures in 1989 to at least 1iC by 1995. This implies an
increase from Z$14 million to about Z860 million a year. For Zimbabwe
as a whole, improved health status of the population will yield
important quality-of-life benefits from large numbers of people, and
will also improve directly the productivity of the adult labor force.
Better child health and natrition will enhance the capacity of
Zimbabwe's children of the 1990s to learn in school, and over the long
run this will lead to substantial further growth of labor productivity
for the country.
B. Risks
6.7         There are two main risks associated with the project.  The
first is that the local construction industry, which has struggled to
cope with the hospital and clinic upgrading program under the first
Family Health Project, will not have adequate capacity to carry out the
rural health infrastructure component under FHP2. In the first project,
construction delays occurred because of nationwide shortages of cement,
bricks, and locally-manufactured items for finishing buildings (e.g.,
windows, door handles, bathroom fixtures, etc.), and because of severe
transport problems that limited the movement of construction materials
to actual building sites. The civil works envisioned under FHP2 are
roughly twice as large as in the first project. To minimize this risk,
actions both within the project and in the larger construction industry
will be taken. The schedule for hospital and RHC upgrading will be
staggered across the five-year implementation period, to avoid
"bunching" of construction activity. Moreover, civil works contracts
will be broken down into several packages and awarded to a number of
construction firms, thus reducing the risk of a major setback if one
firm under-performs. More broadly, the Government is taking steps under
the economic adjustment/trade liberalization program to bolster the
construction industry, by encouraging local and foreign investment in
cement, brick, and other construction materials production and making
additional foreign exchange available for import of manufacturing
equipment. Government and donor efforts to remove the existing
constraints on availability of building supplies and on internal
transport (including World Bank-funded projects for the construction
industry and the national railways) are expected to intensify over the
next 3-5 years.
6.8         The second risk is that MOH and other implementing
Government agencies will not have adequate capacity to plan, manage, and
evaluate project activities under FHP2. Existing systems are weak in a
number of areas, as noted in Chapter 2, and competition with the private



- 68 -
sector for skilled manpower has resulted in the loss of significant
numbers of doctors, nurses, medical technicians, and managers from the
public sector in recent years. To mltigate this risk, the Government is
taking a number of actions, both within the proposed project and outside
of it. To retain more doctors in public service, MOH and the medical
school at the University of Zimbabwe introduced in 1987 a new curriculum
with a strong community medicine orientation, and in 1989 instituted a
new scheme under which graduating doctors serve for five years in
Government health facilities. The provision of good quality housing at
district hospitals under FHPB has already had a dramatic impact on
staffing levels in the rural areas, with large numbers of doctors and
nurses now eager to serve at these upgraded facilities. The large
cohort of secondary school students now graduating in Zimbabwe should
ensure thet training programs for doctors, nurses, midwives, and other
paramedical cadres - many of which are being substantially expanded
under FHP2 - provide adequate numbers of health workers for the country.
Under these circumstances, a moderate flow of health professionals from
the public to the private sectors is both manageable and, perhaps, even
desirable. Finally, one of the major objectives of FHP2 is precisely to
develop the planning and management systems which are lacking in the
health, population, and nutrition sectors, thereby building up local
capacity in these areas. The combination of training, selective
technical assistance, and provision of equipment should help to create
and sustain this capacity over the next five years.



- 69 -
VII.   ASSURANCES AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.1         The following will be a condition for project effectiveness$
(a)   that the two new posts in the Project Management Unit and the
position of the architect/physical planning advisor be filled by
persons with qualifications and experience acceptable to the World
Bank (para. 5.5).
7.2         During negotiations, assurances on the following issues were
given by the Government:
(a)   that ZNFPC will recruit and deploy at least 80 additional
community-based distributors prior to June 30, 1993, and
thereafter, will base its decision on the future recruitment of
CBDs on the results of studies to be completed by the end of 1991
and 1992, respectively (para. 3.10); and MOH will recruit and
deploy 40 additional nutrition cooordinators prior to June 30,
1993 (para. 3.35);
(b)   that EPI/Nutrition/Health surveys will be undertaken in 1991 and
1994 and the results submitted to the Bank by March of each
following year (para. 3.26);
(c)   that, before proceeding to prepare tender documents, MOH will send
final designs for each district hospital to the World Bank for
review (para. 3.45);
(d)   that each province will submit a proposal justifying its choice of
rural health centers for construction or upgrading by December 31,
1991, and these proposals will be forwarded to the World Bank for
review before designs are finalized and tender documents prepared
(para. 3.46);
(e)   that audits of project accounts by the Auditor General or by
independent auditors acceptable to the Bank will be made available
to the Bank within nine months of the close of each fiscal year
(para. 4.21);
(f)   that CMED will by March 31, 1992 provide to MOH and the World Bank
a plan containing its proposed new arrangements for maintenance
and repair of MOH vehicles, and, following consultation with MOH
and the Bank, CMED will implement the agreed plan by July 1, 1992
(para. 5.17);
(g)   that consolidated semi-annual reports of project monitoring data
will be compiled by the Project Management Unit within six weeks
of the end of each semester and forwarded to the World Bank,
following review by the Project Advisory Committee, within eight
weeks of the end of each semester (para. 5.19); and
(h)   that MOH will submit to the World Bank by May 15 of each year the
annual work plan for the following fiscal year (para. 5.21).



- 70 -
7.3         Subject to the above assurance. and conditions, the proposed
project will constitute a suitable basis for an IBRD loan of US$25
million on standard terms to the Government of Zimbabwe.

ZIMBABWE
SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
Introduction To The Five-Year Family Planning Strategy
The Development of a Family Planning Strategy
1.          Donor investment and funding in the FP sector will total more than
US$40 million over the next five years, including the USAID project, the UNFPA
country program and the FHP2 FP component. This amounts to more than three
times ZNFPC's current annual expenditure. Moreover, each of these donor
programs is complex in design, covering a variety of activities across several
sub-sectors and agencies. In addition, there is a consensus that Zimbabwe
needs to move on to a wider and more dynamic phase of FP program-building,
incorporating new approaches in many areas.
2.          In such circumstances, the development of a comprehensive and
integrated strategy for Zimbabwe's family planning program over the next five
years, covering all agencies and donors in the sector, appeared imperative,
and became an essential part of FHP2 preparation. Aided by technical
assistance provided under the Japanese Grant Facility (see para 2.89), ZNFPC
developed an overall national strategy and accompanying sub-strategies for FP
Training, IEC, clinic-based and outreach FP service delivery, Youth Advisory
Services and FP program management; these in turn generated matching
implementation pla;is which formed the basis for preparation of the FHP2
component.  For .nternal scheduling reasons, USAID and UNFPA had already
completed the basic design of their programs, which were thus incorporated
into the strategy as they stood; however both agencies supported and
collaborated with the process of strategy development and indicated their
willingness to adjust their planned activities, as and where appropriate, to
harmonize effectively with its overall thruet.
3.          This national strategy and implementation plan follows in Annex
2:C; the accompanying sub-strategies, which like the national implementation
plan lay out the allocation of each activity between or among donors, can be
found in the Implementation Volume. The national strategy and plan is
conceived, however, as only the first, basic step in a continuing process of
planning and management. Building on it, provincial and district
implementation plans will be developed and embodied in annual workplans; these
annual wr!kplans will in turn take into account changing circumstances and
consequent necessary modifications to the original strategy and implementation
plan. The development of the provincial and district implementation plans is
expected to take place in mid-1991, together with preparation of the first
annual set of workplans. To assure continuity and a sound start to
implementation, technical assistance will be provided under WHP2 to these two
operatious; thEreafter Z."FPC will managp the process with their own resources.



- 83 -
ANNEX 2 PART 2
Page 2 of 5
An Outline of the National PP Strategy
4.          The national PP strategy for 1991-96 sets two goals:
1.    to reduce the national total fertllity rate to 4.5 by 1996;
2.    to increase the financial oustainability of the PP program, while
providing high-quality services and ensuring access to low-income
clients.
Each goal in turn is to be achieved through a set of specified objectives.
5.          The two objectives for Goal 1, Reduction of the TFR, may be
summarized as follows:
-     to increase the prevalence of use of modern PP methods to 48
percent of married women of reproductive age by 1996;
to increase the use of long-term and permanent FP methods to 12
percent of married women of reproductive age by 1996;
6.          The five objectives for Goal 2, Increasing program financial
sustainabillty, may be summarized as follows:
-     to maximize cost effectiveness of services;
-     to recover 25 percent of contraceptive costs and 10 percent of
medical/clinical costs by 1996;
-     to increase PP service delivery by the private sector to 17
percent of all services by 1996;
-     to increase ZNFPC non-FP revenues to recover 10 percent of
training and IEC costs and 100 percent of hostel and restaurant
costs by 1996;
-     to diversify donor funding and increase government allocations.
These objectives are to be attained through a set of specified strategies.
The Relationship of the FHP2 PP Component to the PP Strategy
7.          In the framework of this overall matrix, and taking into account
the broad areas of interest of USAID and UNFPA, as well as the activities to
which USAID and TJNFPA are already committed, the PHP2 FP component will
concentrate on activities falling under both objectives of Goal 1 (increasing
PP prevalence and Increasing use of longer-term methods) and the first and
last objectives of Goal 2 (maxlzising cost-effectiveness of service and
diversifvina donor funding and increasing government allocations).



- 84 -
ANNEX 2 PART I
Page 3 of 5
8.          Under Goal 1 of reducing the TFR and its two objectives of
increasing FP prevalence and increasing use of longer-ternm methods, a number
of strategies are specified under the headings of service delivery, IEC, Youth
Advisory Services, training, evaluation and research and management.
Strategies for service delivery are tot
-     identify target groups
-     carry out research into desires, needs and concerns
-     carry out an inventory of resources
-     expand service capability
-     introduce new methods
The FRP2 FP component will focus particularly on the strategy of expanding
service capability; in keeping with previous Bank approaches in FHP I, support
will be given primarily to activities implemented through MOH/MOLG/City Health
Departments and through ZNFPC.
9.          Strategies for IEC are to:
-     increase demand for services
-     improve provider skills
-     create a more favorable climate of opinion
The FHP2 FP component will provide support to all three strategies.
10.         Strategies for YAS are to:
-     provide information and services to enable youth ti' develop
responsible patterns of reproductive health behaviar
-     assist parents to carry out the role of sex educators
The FHP2 FP component will support both these strategies.
11.         Strategies for training are to:
-     expand training to cover additional service providers
-     make training more relevant to method mix diversification
-     increase trainee follow-up and supervision
-     increase training revenues
The FHP2 FP component will concentrate support on the first three of these
four strategies.
12.         Stratagies for evaluation and research are to:
-     produce regular routine data on service provision for all
providers
-     conduct appropriate research in accordance with program needs
The FHP2 PP component will provide support to both these strategies.



- 85 -
ANNEX 2 PART 2
Page 4 of 5
13.         Strategies for management are tot
-     strengthen ZNFPC management structure and procedures
-     strengthen ZNFPC planning procedures
-     strengthen ZNFPC financial management
-     support the provision of contraceptive commodities
-     strengthen the development of policy and
coordination
The FHP2 FP component will focus support on the first three of these five
strategies.
14.         Under Goal 2's first objective of maximizing cost-effectiveness of
services, a number of strategies are specified as follows:
-     research on cost-effectiveness of alternative delivery strategies
-     continued expansion of services through increased use of existing
resources to achieve economies of scale
-     review and implementation of studies on local contraceptive
commodity production
-     study of the cost-effectiveness of training
-     development and implementation of management improvements in
decentralized planning, budgeting, financial control and cost
accounting
-     review of the most cost-effective use and management of transport
-     development of a system for monitoring quality of services
-     review and improvement of the service statistics system
-     provision of logistics management support
-     cost-effectiveness study of IEC materials production methods and
different media
The FHP2 PP component will focus particularly on the strategy concerning
increased use of existing resources (particularly of government health
facilities, Youth Advisory Services and IEC facilities), and on the group of
management-related strategies concerning improvements in decentralized
management, quality indicators and monitoring through service statistics. In
addition, the PHP2 Strengthening Rural Health Service Delivery component will
provide support to increased use of existing resources, through constructior.
of an MCH/FP district training center and purpose-built PP clinic in each of
the 16 district hospitals due to be upgraded under this component. With regard
to local commodity production, a project through IFC is being considered for
local production of oral contraceptives.



- 86 -
ANNEX 2 PART 2
Page 5 of 5
15.         Under Goal 2's last objective of diversifying donor funding and
lncreasfing government allocatLons, a number of strategies are specified as
followst
-     developing additional sources of funds from new and current
donors, in particular for contraceptive supplies
-     negotiating with MFEPD for increased budgetary allocations for
contraceptive supplies
-     negotiating with government and donors for the provision of
foreign exchange for FP supplies
-     having government take over an increased share of the cost of oral
contraceptives
-     having government and non-USAID donors take over an increased
share of the cost of purchasing contraceptives other than orals
and NORPLANT
-     promoting fund-raising from private and corporate donors
-     strengthening ability to manage donor-funded projects
The FHP2 FP component will directly su>-port the first of these strategies,
through its mobilization of co-financing for the component from bilateral and
multilateral donors either already funding FP activities under FHP I or new to
the PP sector in Zimbabwe. It will alsn indirectly contribute to the last
through its support to management strengthening in ZNFPC, and to several of
the others through its general support to a long-tern enlargem3nt of the
country's FP program and its provision of back-up funds for purchase of
contraceptive supplies in the case of emergency shortages.

ZIMBABWE
SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
Draft Outline of Terms of Reference
For Local and Overseas Consultancies
For ZNFPC Management Review
The Background to the Review
1.          The Zimbabwe National Family Planning Council (ZNFPC) was set up
in 1984 to coordinate and manage a family planning program which at that time
consisted largely of the ZNFPC's own clinic and outreach services, and catered
to less than 30 percent of the married women of reproductive age in Zimbabwe.
Since then, the government health service network has become the major
supplier of FP services in Zimbabwe ( catering to between 50 and 60 percent of
FP clients), and ZNFPC's own role has shifted more and more towards training,
referral and t'<^nical coordination and support. This shift is d&9 to
intensify sti:' -arther under the soon-to-be-implemented 5-year kerategy for
expansion and .pgrading of the country's FP program. This strategy provides
not only for a substantial expansion of the existing FP program, but also for
further decentralization of ZNFPC management to enlarged ZNFPC provincial
offices.
2.          The time iaas come, therefore, to review the structure and
management of ZNFPC in the light of past and future developments in ZNFPCs
role and functions. Such a review is an essential element of the initial stage
of strategy operationalisation, and its purpose is to ensure that ZNFPC can
make its full contribution to strategy implementation.
Composition, Structure and Length of the Consultancy
3.          The team conducting the management review will be composed of two
local management consultants, of whom one will be a specialist in financial
management and the other in general management, plus an overseas consultant
specialist in family planning program management. This team will carry out a
full marhagement audit and review of the ZNFPC, covering every level --
headquarters, provincial offices and field workforce -- of the organization.
The team will report to the Acting Executive Director of the ZNFPC. The
assignment will last approximately 4 weeks including report writing, and will
comprise time in ZNFPC headquarters, visits to all provincial offices and
selected field trips to meet field staff. The final report, including
findings, analysis of issues, conclusions and recomuendations, will be
submitted to the Acting Executive Director in draft form at the end of the
period spent on site, and in full no longer than 2 weeks after submission of
the draft.



- 89 -
ANNEX 2 PART 4
Page 2 of 2
Objective and ScoPe of the Review
4.          The overall objectives of the management review are (a) to review
and assess the appropriateness of ZNPPC's current structure, size, and
management and administrative policies and practices, relative to the tasks
assigned to ZNFPC under both the Act of Parliament that established it and the
forthcoming
national '-year FP strategy; and (b) to make recommendations for appropriate
modificat.ons in the light of those tasks. Particular attention will be paid
to the identification of any management-related or organizational constraints
or bottlenecks hampering ZNFPC in the performance of its assigned tasks,
together with possible measures to remove them.
5.          Specifically, the review will cover the following topics, all at
central, provincial office and field level and in relation to ZNFPC's role,
functions and activities:
-     ZNFPC's organizational structure
-     ZNIPC's staffing and salary levels and structure
-     ZRlPPCa management, administrative and personnel policies and
practices
-     ZNFPC's financial and budgetary policies and practices

NUTRITION, FP AND MCH COMPONENTSt HEALTH EDUCATION/IEC
TERMS OF REFERENCE FOR IEC CONSULTANT
ROLE
A well qualified communication expert will be required to provide
support and training to the staff of the Ministry of Health and ZNFPC
involved in health education. This includes staff in HEU, Nutrition,
and ZNFPC. Several periodic visits of about one month each are
envisaged during the project period. The consultant will report to the
Director, MCH/FP through Unit heads.
QUALIFICATIONS
Masters Degree in healt'h education or equivalent with at least five
years of field experience--preferably in Africa. Demonstrated skills in
manpower development, program planning, project management, IEC
materials development, formative and operational research and
evaluation. Specialization in IEC for primary health care, MCH family
planning, child survival, nutrition, environmental health, or control of
diarrhoea diseases.
TASKS TO BE PERFORMED
The consultant will assist the Health Education Unit to prioritize and
select from the following list of tasks for each of several periodic
visits to the countrys
1.    Assess the training needs in IEC for staff of ZNFPC, Health
Education and Nutrition programs at central and provincial levels.
2.    Develop training design based on assessed needs and requirements
for Family Health Project Phase II IEC. Support and conduct in-
service training for Nutrition, Health Education and ZNFPC staff.
3.    Consult with the appropriate staff at the provincial and district
levels to strengthen the IEC components of the pilot districts'
FHP II project proposals.
4.    Develop standardized protocol for conducting IEC activities.
5.    Develop training design and conduct training of trainers workshop
for discrict research teams.
6.    Develop training design for training NGOs and other outreach
workers in IEC/social mobilization at the district level.
7.    Assist in the formulation of an agreement for MOH/ZNFPC sharing of
IEC production facilities at the Zimbabwe National Family Planning
Council and monitor utilization.



- 92 -
Annex 3 Part 2
Page 2 of 2
8.    Develop guidelines for planning district Family Health Project II
IEC campaigns/programs.
9.    Participate in designing and conducting a comprehensive evaluation
of the Diploma in Health and Adult Education Course at the
University of Zimbabwe.
10.   Provide technical assistance to update and improve the DHEA course
and participate in teaching the IEC state-of-the-art modules.
DURATION:   Four to six months in periodic visits.
******** ********** ****** **** ** *******
TASKS WHICH WILL REQUIRE TECHNICAL SUPPORT IN I.E.C. FOR
NUTRITION COMPONENT OF THE FAMILY HEALTH PROGRAM
--------------------------------------------------------------__--
To the extent that the consultant cannot include the following tasks in
his/her terms of reference, either because of time limitations or
because the consultant does not have relevant very specific experience,
additional consultants will be hired tot
1.   Assess training needs in IEC for Nutrition Department and workers
in other sectors who are involved in the nutrition programme.
2.    Develop a training strategy which will facilitate the transfer of
skills and expertise in IEC for nutrition activities. (In this
regard it is envisaged that the focus will be on training of
trainers.)
3.    Develop and produce guidelines for field implementation and
coordination of community-based IEC activities in nutrition.
4.    Develop an integrated community based and participatory IEC
strategy for nutrition, based on social marketing concepts and
techniques using social science research advertising and marketing
techniques in order to achieve the desired behavioral change which
would lead to improved nutrition.
5.    Improve the IEC components of the Co-munity Nutrition Programme
through the development and production of 1000 nutrition kits
consisting of flip chart and nutrition manuals for community level
workers.
6.   Develop, implement and evaluate a national social marketing
campaign on infant and young child feeding.

Consultancy for the Directorate of !Juraint Services
Ministry of Health, Zimbabwe
to Explore the Need for
Training for SCNS with Sgecial Learning Needs
BACKGROUND
A substantial but undefined number of health workers who serve in
rural health centers in Zimbabwe are former combatants who have entered
the health service after the way of independence and serve an State
Certified Nurses. The nursing officers have stressed the imAportance of
this group in staffing rural health facilities and would like them to
have more opportunities to upgrade their nualifications *.o midwifery
nurse.  Unfortunately a large prope,_.6on of theee staff have been
unsuccessful in efforts to upgrade their qualifications through
participation in formal training. The nursing Directorate is proposing
that a study be conducted to determine the magnitude of this problem and
to recommend training solutions-
PURPOSEs Conduct a study to determine the feasibility of conducting
remedial training for SCN ex-combatants who wish to prepare for SCMN
certification.
DURATIONs 2 months
Starting Date: April-May 1993
CONSULTANT QUALIFICATIONS:
Minimum of Masters Degree in Nursing Education/Research/Curriculum
Design;
Fluent or able to communicate freely in English, Shona and
Ndebele;
Experienced in data collection and analysis resulting in
formulation of a plan;
Experience in needs analysis and curriculum design.
TERMS OF REFERENCE:
1.    Conduct a needs assessment of ex-combatants to determine
their interest in, and ability to participate in a remedial
course to prepare for SCMN training.

training ex-comhatante and recommend training strategies to
prepare this cadre for formal training.

CONSULTANCY FOR THE DIRECTORATE OF NURSING SERVICES
MINISTRY OF HEALTH, ZIMBABWE
TO
DEVELOP PILOT DISTANCE LEARNING PROGRAM FOR NURSES AND MIDWIVES
PURPOSE t
Work with the Nursing Directorate and School of Post-Basic Nursing
Education to develop a pilot study program to provide 250 nurses serving
in rural areas with learning materials and tutorial support. Design ways
to evaluate the pilot program, so as to be able to determine the
effectiveness of distance learning as an alternative to the traditional
workshop and seminar approach.
BACKGROUND:
Additional continuing education opportunities are required for nurses
working in more remote areas. Traditional inservice training ceminars and
workshops are an expensive means of supporting sta- development and are
frequently under-utilized by nurses who are assigned to ruras. health
centers. The nursing division has identified these nurses and midwives as
an important,  under-served group that requires   knowledge and skill
supplementation on a regular basis. The Nursing Department proposes the
development and testing of a pilot program in distance learning to address
the need for updated knowledge and skills among nurses who serve in the
rural areas. The distance learn program modules should provide regular
updates  on both  technical  and management  issues.    Proposed  topics
include; clinic management, supervision and technical issues related to
antenatal, delivery and postnatal care and family planning as well as
cur-ent information about care and nutrition of young children.
DURATIO}J: 3 months
LIKELY TIMING: 2 months in 1993, 1 month in 1994
CONSULTANT QUALIFICATIONS:
Minimum of Masters Degree ia Nursing Education/Midwifery;
Experience in research, curriculum design and distance learning
material development;
Fluent or able to communicate freely in English.
Understanding of conditions of service of nurses working in rural
health centers and clinics.



- 102 -
ANNEX 3 PART 6
Page 2 of 2
TERMS OF REFERENCEs
1.    Work with the program coordinator and School of Post Basic Nursing
Education to:
(a)   decide on content and design of course;
(b)   work  out  logistics  of  program  management  including
distribution of modules, student monitoring and follow-up;
(c)   carry out a preliminary needs assessment study to determine
and pretest the content and reading level for written learning
modules materials;
(d)   design and develop learning modules to address the needs of
nurses and midwives rural practice;
(e)   devise an appropriate plan for evaluation of the distance
learning program.
2.    Meet with the Directorate of Nursing Service at least weekly to
discuss progress and plans, and prepare and submit a final report to
the Directorata before leaving the country.

ZIMBA1dW  SECON   AIL    ELT   R]
Study on Matemal Mortality
Background
The Government of Zimbabwe has fundamentally changed the health policy of the
Pre-independence period when health services were largely curative and urban based. The
new policy advocates the adoption of the PLI' approach in order to make health services
accessible to majority of the people. Consequently there has been a significant improvement
in the coverage of services to pregnant women; with more than 90% of expectant mothers
attending antenatal clinics and about 709S delivering in Health facilities. Although it is still
difficult to get a reliable estimate of maternal mortality rate, available information indicates
that maternal mortality rate has hardly changed since independence. Direct and indirect
obstetric causes and other associated factors leading to maternal mortality in Zimbabwe seem
similar to the rest of the African continent yet Zimbabwe's maternal mortality rate at 90 per
100,000 live births according to the World Health Organization is much lower than the
regional average, this figure is based on births taking place in health facilities, hence not
representative of maternal deaths in the community particularly in rural areas.
Objectives
1.    To establish more accurate alternative methods of estimating maternal
mortality rate.
2.    To determine the complexity, magnitude and consequences of the problem of
maternal deaths.
3.    To determine the impact or role of the various complex inter-related factors
responsible for maternal deaths e.g. social, cultural economic and medico-
legal.
4.    To assess the adequacy or inadequacy of the current resources in the health
care system devoted to meeting the needs of women in order to reduce the risk
of pregnancy related deaths.
1.    The study will attempt to address the following:
(a)    dimension of the problem in Zimbabwe bearing in mind the
rural/urban and regional differentials.
(b)    complex inter-related factors concerning the status of women e.g.
income, employment education, health fertility and women's roles.
(c)    abortion and the law.
(d)   the role of health services in preventing maternal deaths.



- 105 -                          ANNEX 3  PART 8
Page 2 of 2
The approach would entail the following:
(a)   identification of the neglected needs of women as evidenced by high
levels of maternal mortality.
(b)    better and more ef.ecient use of existing resources - personnel,
facilities drugs and supplies and operational support services.
(c)   allocation of more resources to meet the needs of women.
(d)   design of appropriate, effective and efficient muernal health care
systems.

ZIMBABWE SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT                                Page 1 of 2
Study on Impact of In-Service Training:
Backgound
Personnel remain the core need. An insufficient mber, inadequate and sometimes
inappropriate training of existing workers continue to be a major constraints on the
development of the health services. Substantial resources have therefore been devoted to the
training of the most critical cadres in a variety of areas. The first and second Family Health
Projects provide for an ambitious in-service training program covering thousands of person
weeks of training locally and overseas. The training has been very comprehensive in scope
covering personnel at National Provincial and district levels; and in areas as varied as
financial management health and manpower planning, PHC management and Supervision in
midwifery, family planning, drug supply maintenance and repair.
The output of such a training program may be measured, among others in the nu mber
of person weeks. However, a study is necessary to evaluate the impact of in-service training
on the quality, efficiency and effectiveness of the overall health care delivery system.
Objectives
1.    To assess the training carried out in terms of achievement of targets, and
quality of training.
2.    To evaluate ex-trainees' capabilities achieved as a result of training.
3.    To asses the impact of the training within the context of wider programme
objectives and target as set out in the SAR.
Scope
1.    What was actually done in the ln-service  ining project?
Assescurriculum, content, materials' development, teaching methods and
management (physical targets met, courses held, materials developed, etc).
Critical indicators
(a,     r Inee/days of training conducted (no. trainees x no. of days of
training).
(b)    Training Materl developed or distributed. Person weks.
(c)    Teaching Methods used. This, if coupled with a assessment of
percentage of te-r Ing time given to practical work, is another good
indicator of quality oi training.
2.    How much and what did trainees ..rn?
Assess the amount of informatlon, skills, etc that trainees gained and retained
after training.
Establish the DIRECT impact of the training on the intended trainees; what changes
Oearning) occurred in them as a result of the training conducted.



- 107 -                          ANNEX 3  PART 9
Page 2 of 2
Critical Indicators
(a)   Improvement In scores on pre/posttests conducted at training
courses.
(b)    Delayed post-training evaluation of knowledge and skill gain and
retntion.
(c)    Subjective assessment of training relevance and needs by potental
and x-trainees.
3.    How are things being done now In the fleld?
Assess improved quality and/or increased quntity of performance of ex-trainees in
their work after training. Also look at extended coverage, increase in client load,
increased CPR, lowered discontinuation, increased use of ORS, etc.
Critical Indicators
(a)    Worker awareness of the impact of other constraints.
(b)    Actual Improvements In quaUty or quantity of work when other
constraints are controlled:
(c)    Appropriate training content and design:
Anreh _
The approach would entail the kinds of acivities:
1.    Review of training programme: study of documentation, interviews with
trainers, observation of training situations, courses, etc.
2.    Questionnaires, Ierviews, evauation tests, with a sample of ex-trainees.
3.    Performance Asessment/Task Analysis of a sample of workers in working
situatons.
